(2023), Margetts et al. (2024), and Nazzal et al. (2024) compared the time and \naccuracy of writing literature review articles using three methods, AI-only, AI-assisted, and human-only, and \nfound that while the AI-only method took the least time to complete, it had the lowest accuracy and required \nsubstantial time for revision. Recent AI literature review assistants such as \u201cAI Scholar\u201d and \u201cElicit\u201d can be used \nto overcome previous limitations. For instance, a study by Bond et al. (2024) reported that combining ChatGPT \nand  Elicit  to  conduct  a  literature  review  can  streamline  the  review  process  by  quickly  identifying  and \nexamining specific sections of relevant studies.  \n\nResearch drafting and language support \n\nThe basic capabilities of GAI show that these tools efficiently assist in the preliminary stages of writing, for \ninstance, they  enable  researchers to  produce  initial  drafts  with  greater  speed  compared  to  the  traditional \nwriting process (Abuyaman, 2023; Alkaissi & McFarlane, 2023; Firat, 2023; Leong, 2023; Lozi\u0107 & \u0160tular, 2023; \n\nOnline Journal of Communication and Media Technologies, 15(1), e202502 \n\n9 / 20 \n\n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n\fSalman et al. \n\nMahyoob et al., 2023; Semrl et al., 2023; Zheng et al., 2023). This allows researchers to dedicate more time to \ncritical thinking and analysis rather than focusing on writing techniques. In addition, Ariyaratne et al. (2023) \nand Margetts et al. (2024) explored how GAI technologies are continuously evolving and provide more services \nto researchers, such as helping to draft complete  parts  of  manuscripts  like  introductions  and  conclusions. \nThis  suggests  a  shift  from  simple  drafting  tools  to  more  comprehensive  writing  assistance.  Furthermore, \nseveral studies reported services like enhancing existing texts in terms of writing style, grammar, and overall \ntext  coherence  which  improves  the  readability  and  outreach  of  scientific  work  to  a  wider  audience \n(Anghelescu et al., 2023; Athaluri et al., 2023; Bond et al., 2024; Dashti et al., 2023; Livberber, 2023; Margetts \net  al.,  2024;  Nguyen  et  al.,  2024).  Bekker  (2024)  discussed  the  advantages  of  GAI  for  non-native  English \nscholars by providing translation services as an added benefit.  \n\nHowever,  with  this  expanded  role  in  manuscript  documentation  comes  the  need  for  careful  human \noversight (Nazzal et al., 2024; Perkins & Roe, 2023) since these tools can provide fabricated content (M\u00e1jovsk\u00fd \net al., 2023) and inaccurate scientific content based on biased data (Mahyoob et al., 2023). In other words, \nwhile  GAI  can  efficiently  produce  complete  drafts,  these  documents  require  substantial  human  input  to \nensure they meet the rigorous standards of accuracy and authenticity expected in scholarly work. In addition, \nusers who actively interact with GAI tools achieve better academic writing (Nguyen et al., 2024). Khlaif et al. \n(2023) emphasized the importance of detailed \u201cprompt engineering\u201d to enhance the factual accuracy of the \ngenerated output.  \n\nData analysis  \n\nSeveral studies in different domains indicated that GAI tools are increasingly utilized in various aspects of \ndata analysis and interpretation, enabling researchers to perform tasks such as statistical data analysis, and \ndata  visualization,  in  addition  to  thematic  analysis,  ChatGPT  was  found  to  simplify  the  process  for  novice \nresearchers to comprehend and manipulate datasets through a user-friendly analysis tool (Abdelhafiz et al., \n2024;  Burger  et  al.,  2023;  Crawford  et  al.,  2023;  Datt  et  al.,  2023;  Firat,  2023;  Morocco-Clarke  et  al.,  2023; \nPraveen & Vajrobol, 2023). In addition, GAI tools can be used by researchers to create sample datasets and \nassist in text classification tasks (Ebert & Louridas, 2023). In qualitative research, GAI tools enhance analysis \nby streamlining coding processes and assisting with the initial exploration of datasets (Dengel et al., 2023; Yan \net al., 2023). Some researchers tested ChatGPT\u2019s  potential  in  predictive  modeling.  For  example, a  study by \nZheng et al. (2023) reported ChatGPT\u2019s ability to predict how certain chemicals are made by synthesizing data \nfrom 228 papers.  \n\nHowever,  other  studies  reported  the  necessity  to  review  generated  content  due  to  the  possibility  of \ngenerating inaccurate  output  (Semrl  et al.,  2023),  for  example,  Biswas  et  al.  (2023)  tested  the  capability  of \nusing  ChatGPT  to  analyze  medical  case  reports  and  found  that  it  lacked  synthesizing  complex  cases. \nAdditionally,  in  their  2023  study,  Zheng  et  al.  (2023)  highlighted  two  main  challenges:  the  occurrence  of \n\u201challucinations\u201d where the AI generates fabricated information, and the need for effective prompt engineering \nto guide the AI\u2019s responses accurately. \n\nReference support \n\nIn evaluating the effectiveness of GAI tools such as ChatGPT, Bard, and Bing in References and citation \nsupport, various studies have reported limitations in this task. As reported by multiple studies these tools, \nwhile beneficial in enhancing manuscript documentation and language assistance, often fall short of providing \naccurate references. Many studies reported inaccurate reference generation by these tools (Anghelescu et al., \n2023; Ariyaratne et al., 2023; Athaluri et al., 2023; Livberber, 2023; Margetts et al., 2024; Seth et al., 2023; Wu \n& Dang, 2023). For example, a study by Aiumtrakul et al. (2023) in medical research evaluated the accuracy of \nreferences generated from ChatGPT-3.5, Bing Chat, and Bard in different nephrology topics and revealed that \nthe  references  generated  included  a  mix  of  correct  and  incorrect,  with  some  being  incomplete  or  entirely \nfabricated.  These  studies  emphasize  the  importance  of  a  detailed  review  by  researchers  to  ensure  the \naccuracy  of  AI-generated  references  and  citations.  However,  the  latest  GAI  research  plug-ins  such  as \n\u201cConsensus\u201d and \u201cScholar GPT\u201d which are based on the GPT model do provide access to databases such as \nGoogle Scholar, PubMed, JSTOR, etc.  \n\n10 / 20 \n\nOnline Journal of Communication and Media Technologies, 15(1), e202502 \n\n \n \n\fOnline Journal of Communication and Media Technologies, 2025 \n\nTable 6. Concerns and limitations discussed on GAI use \nConcern/limitation \n1. Accuracy and bias \n\nDetails \n\n\u2212  Data erroneous \n\u2212  Outdated data \n\u2212  Fabricated data \n\u2212  Biased data \n2. Academic integrity \n\nFactual inaccuracies, incorrect or non-relevant content \nNon-recent training data \nNon-existing, made-up content \nSkewed or unfair content \n\n\u2212  Plagiarism and originality \n\u2212  Ethical standards and verification \n\nPotential for AI to produce plagiarized or insufficiently original content \nLack of robust ethical guidelines and verification processes to ensure the \noriginality and integrity of AI-generated content \n\n3. Reliance on GAI \n\n\u2212 \n\n\u2212 \n\u2212 \n\nImpact on research skills \n\nReduced development of critical research skills and lower research quality \ndue to AI dependency \n\nImpact on innovation and creativity  Decreased innovation and creative thinking as reliance on GAI tools \nIncreased workload \n\nExpectations of more work from academics given the existence of GAI tools \n\n4. Data privacy \n\n\u2212  Data misuse by GAI tools providers  Risks of data being used unethically, including privacy breaches and misuse \n\n\u2212  Vulnerability to cyber-attacks \n\nof personal data \nData breaches, and unauthorized access by cyber-attackers \n\nPeer review  \n\nThe discussion of integrating GAI in the peer review process is growing across the academic community. \nVarious studies discussed the role of ChatGPT that could play in streamlining this critical process (Biswas et \nal., 2023; Carabantes et al., 2023; Checco et al., 2021; Saad et al., 2024). Findings indicate that it can perform \ntasks  like  automating  initial  screenings  and  assessing  the  quality  of  manuscripts  which  could  significantly \nlighten the load on reviewers. Additionally, there is potential to provide feedback on manuscripts (Checco et \nal.,  2021)  and  maintain  structural  and  content  quality  with  academic  standards  (Abdelhafiz  et  al.,  2024; \nZohouri  et  al.,  2024).  Despite  these  advantages,  the  current  technical  limitations  of  the  GAI  tools  cannot \nreplace  human  reviewers  due  to  the  lack  of  understanding  of  complex  contexts  (Carabantes  et  al.,  2023; \nZohouri et al., 2024). In addition, the practice of using these tools by reviewers underscores the necessity for \nclear guidelines and policies, as argued by Biswas et al. (2023) and Mollaki (2024).  \n\nMoreover,  with  AI\u2019s  growing  presence  in  peer  review,  establishing  a  framework  for  its  use  is  crucial  to \nensure that it augments rather than undermines the integrity of the scholarly review process. Furthermore, \nconcerns about bias and the ability of reviewers to distinguish between AI and human-generated texts require \ndeveloping effective AI detection tools that can be adopted in the peer review process (Checco et al., 2021). \n\nRQ3. What Are the Main Areas of Concern and Limitations Reported by Researchers \nRegarding the Use of GAI Tools? \n\nThis study also identified the main areas of researchers\u2019 concerns and GAI tools limitations associated with \ntheir use in academic research and peer review and classified them into four categories (Table 6). \u201cAccuracy \nand bias\u201d, this aspect includes reported issues related to the accuracy of data generated and subdivided into \nerroneous, outdated, fabricated, and biased data. The second concern, \u201cacademic integrity and ethical use\u201d \nrelates to concerns related to the ethical use of GAI tools like plagiarism and originality, transparent use, and \nprivacy of data.  \n\nConcerns related to the impact on researchers\u2019  skills  and  research  innovation  were grouped  under  the \n\u201cdependence on AI\u201d theme. The fourth aspect discussed by researchers was the \u201cdata privacy and security\u201d of \nGAI tools and subdivided into, handling of complex data and context window size. \n\nAccuracy and bias \n\nFindings indicate that the \u201caccuracy and bias\u201d of the GAI-generated content is a significant concern among \nresearchers. This aspect is related to data erroneous like factual inaccuracies and irrelevant content which is \na highly threatening factor to academic research standards (Abdelhafiz et al., 2024; Abuyaman, 2023; Alkaissi \n& McFarlane, 2023; Bond et al., 2024). It was also reported that GAI tools can provide \u201coutdated data\u201d due to \n\nOnline Journal of Communication and Media Technologies, 15(1), e202502 \n\n11 / 20 \n\n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n\fSalman et al. \n\naccess  restrictions to  recent  scholarly  work  and  digital  databases  (Abuyaman,  2023;  M\u00e1jovsk\u00fd  et  al.,  2023; \nMohammed Alyasiri et al., 2024). Other authors reported fabricated non-existent generated content by GAI \ntools known as \u201cAI Hallucinations\u201d which is a significant challenge in GAI development because it undermines \ntheir reliability and trustworthiness (Alkaissi & McFarlane, 2023; Carabantes et al., 2023; Firat, 2023; Mahyoob \net al., 2023; Schmidt et al., 2023; Zheng et al., 2023).  \n\nAnother concern raised is that GAI-generated content can be biased. This issue can occur if the training \ndata lacks diverse perspectives which can lead to biased output threatening academic inclusivity (Abuyaman, \n2023;  Hake  et  al.,  2024;  Mollaki,  2024;  Perkins  &  Roe,  2023).  Generated  content  Inaccuracies  occur  due to \ntechnical  limitations  of  GAI Tools.  These  studies  highlighted  issues  where  AI  tools  struggle  to  process  and \ninterpret  complex  datasets  or  non-standard  formats,  limiting  their  effectiveness  in  fields  requiring  deep \ncontextual understanding. Additionally, their limitation to processing large amounts of data due to the context \nwindow  size  affects  their  efficiency  in  logical  reasoning  tasks  as  discussed  by  Carabantes  et  al.  (2023). \nFurthermore,  GAI  models,  which  rely  on  deep  neural  networks,  lack  explainability  because  their  decision-\nmaking processes are complex and difficult to interpret. \n\nAcademic integrity \n\nAnother significant concern is the potential  for  GAI  tools to  compromise  academic  integrity.  Numerous \nstudies  have  underscored  significant  issues  concerning  plagiarized  academic  content,  whether  it  is  done \nintentionally or unintentionally by researchers. This could happen if the GAI output closely resembles existing \nscientific material without proper acknowledgment or attribution (Crawford et al., 2023; Jarrah et al., 2023; \nLivberber, 2023; Margetts et al., 2024; Zohouri et al., 2024). These studies suggested the formation of policies \nand guidelines to govern and promote responsible and ethical use of GAI tools in academic research workflow \nand peer review processes to maintain the integrity of the scholarly work and the development of robust AI-\ntext detection models to help identify plagiarized text effectively (Abdelhafiz et al., 2024; Mollaki, 2024; Perkins \n& Roe, 2023).  \n\nReliance on GAI \n\nOver-reliance on GAI tools in academic research raised concerns about its impact on research skills and \ncreativity. Some studies suggest that while GAI can enhance efficiency, it may diminish critical thinking and \ninnovative  capacities  among  researchers  by  providing  easy  solutions  to  complex  problems  (Checco  et  al., \n2021; Livberber, 2023; Nguyen et al., 2024; Praveen & Vajrobol, 2023). Nguyen et al. (2024) and Zohouri et al. \n(2024)  further  warns  of  the  potential  for  GAI  to  lead  researchers  to  depend  too  heavily  on  automated \nprocesses,  which  could  negatively  affect  genuine  scholarly  inquiry  and  innovation.  In  addition,  as  GAI \nadvances, many researchers feel diminished by its capabilities which raises worries regarding the redundancy \nof the workforce. This anxiety comes from the fear that GAI tools might exceed human creativity which leads \nto  psychological  stress  and  potentially  hinders  human  innovation  leading  to  what  is  called  \u201ccreative \ndisplacement  anxiety\u201d.  This  concern  can  reduce  researchers\u2019  acceptance  of  GAI  technology  and  lower \nadoption  levels.  Moreover, while  GAI tools  can  handle  repetitive  tasks,  freeing  up  time  for  academics,  this \ntime savings might lead institutions to expect more output including academic research (Watermeyer et al., \n2023).  \n\nData privacy  \n\nMany  studies  raised  concerns  about  the  security  risks  to  data  privacy.  Providers  of  GAI  services  could \njeopardize users\u2019 privacy by mishandling their  data. This danger  is  especially  evident when  using GAI tools \nmay  lead  to  data  breaches  that  expose  sensitive  information.  In  addition,  GAI  systems  are  increasingly \nvulnerable to cyber-attacks. These systems can be targeted in different ways such as introducing harmful data \nor  exploiting  system  weaknesses  to  gain  unauthorized  access.  The  ethical  and  security  considerations \nsurrounding  GAI  tools  are  significant  in  academic  research  and  peer  review  processes  and  need  to  be \naddressed to ensure the confidentiality of researchers\u2019 scholarly work (Alshami et al., 2023; Anghelescu et al., \n2023; Bond et al., 2024; Mollaki, 2024; Nguyen et al., 2024).  \n\n12 / 20 \n\nOnline Journal of Communication and Media Technologies, 15(1), e202502 \n\n \n \n\fOnline Journal of Communication and Media Technologies, 2025 \n\nFigure 7. Measures for effective and ethical GAI use (Source: Authors) \n\nRQ4. What Measures Would Benefit the Academic Research Community to Utilize GAI Tools \nBetter and Overcome Their Limitations? \n\nFrom the studies examined, several key measures have been identified to ensure the effective and ethical \nuse  of  GAI  tools.  Figure  7  illustrates  a  comprehensive  view  of  these  measures  to  enhance  the  use  of \ngenerative  GAI  tools  within  the  academic  research  community.  It  is  organized  into  three  concentric  circles \nrepresenting  key  stakeholder  groups:  regulatory  bodies  and  academic  institutions,  researchers  and \nreviewers, and GAI tools providers. Each group  is  assigned  specific  measures that  address  critical  concern \nareas.  For  regulatory  bodies  and  academic  institutions,  the  measures  include  policies  and  guidelines \ndevelopment,  training  and  AI  literacy,  and  infrastructure  and  resources  development.  Researchers  and \nreviewers  are  focused  on  ethical  standards  and  verification,  collaborative  research  as  well  as  skill \ndevelopment and innovation. GAI tools providers are responsible for transparency and accountability in their \ndevelopment practices, data privacy and security, and model refinement. These stakeholders must work with \neach other not in isolation to achieve effective outcomes and enhance utilization levels while minimizing risks \nand concerns.  \n\nAcademic institutions and regulatory bodies \n\nPolicy  and  regulations  development:  Academic  institutions  and  regulatory  bodies  such  as  higher \neducation, research institutions, publishers, and related government agencies need to establish clear policies \nto  regulate  the  use  of  GAI  tools  in  research.  These  policies  should  comprehensively  address  roles  and \nresponsibilities, ethical use, and guide researchers on the appropriate use of GAI. Key components of these \npolicies  should  include  verification  procedures  guidelines  and  requirements  for  transparency  and \naccountability (Abdelhafiz et al., 2024; Alkaissi & McFarlane, 2023; Jarrah et al., 2023; Mollaki, 2024). Moreover, \nthese  policies  should  be  reviewed  and  updated  regularly  to  reflect  continuous  advancements  in  GAI \ntechnology (Livberber, 2023; Perkins & Roe, 2023; Praveen & Vajrobol, 2023).  \n\nTraining  and  AI  literacy:  The  second  measure  emphasizes  the  importance  of  providing  training  and \nenhancing researchers\u2019 AI literacy to equip them with the necessary skills to better utilize GAI tools. This could \ninclude AI skills development programs and  ethical research  workshops.  Studies  emphasized the  need  for \ntraining that focuses on ethical use (Abdelhafiz et al., 2024; Al-Zahrani, 2023). Nguyen et al. (2024) highlighted \nthe importance of training strategies that meet the specific needs of researchers. For instance, some studies \ndiscussed the benefits of prompt engineering training to generate the most accurate and relevant responses \nwhich improves the precision and reliability of GAI outputs (Khlaif et al., 2023; Mahyoob et al., 2023; Zheng et \nal., 2023).  \n\nOnline Journal of Communication and Media Technologies, 15(1), e202502 \n\n13 / 20 \n\n \n \n \n \n\fSalman et al. \n\nResource support: Findings highlight the significance of academic institutions and government entities \ndedicating resources to advancing and incorporating AI tools into academic research. This involves allocating \nfunds  and  infrastructure  support  for  research  programs  by  establishing  AI  research  hubs.  Academic \ninstitutions are also required to promote collaborative research from different disciplines to foster ethical and \neffective use (Al-Zahrani, 2023; Jarrah et al., 2023; Mollaki, 2024; Perkins & Roe, 2023). Additionally, providing \nAI-assisted research tools including GAI detection tools for researchers in academic can help enhance overall \nthe quality and productivity of academic research (Jarrah et al., 2023; Mollaki, 2024; Perkins & Roe, 2023). \n\nGAI providers \n\nTransparency and accountability: GAI providers must prioritize both transparency and accountability. \nTransparency involves clearly explaining how AI models are developed and function. This practice will foster \ntrust among researchers and reviewers toward the integration of these tools into academic research and peer \nreview. In addition, AI companies must establish systems to monitor the use of their GAI tools to ensure that \nthey  are  used  ethically.  This  includes  setting  up  measures  to  detect  and  prevent  unethical  practices  like \nplagiarism and acting if misuse occurs. By holding themselves accountable, GAI providers ensure their tools \nsupport responsible usage (Yan et al., 2023). \n\nData privacy and security: Providers should follow ethical standards when handling sensitive data of GAI \nusers. This includes enforcing data usage policies and robust security protocols for data protection through \nmethods  such as  advanced encryption,  regular  security  checks,  and  compliance  with  data  protection  laws \n(Anghelescu et al., 2023; Jarrah et al., 2023). \n\nAI  model  refinement:  GAI  providers  must  conduct  refinement  audits  of  their  models  because  this \nprocess  addresses  biases  and  enhances  the  accuracy  and  reliability  of  AI-generated  outputs.  Refinement \naudits  involve  evaluating  both  the  training  data  and  algorithm  performance  which  helps  in  detecting  and \nmitigating  biases  that  may  be  embedded  within  the  data  or  the  algorithms  themselves  (Abuyaman,  2023; \nAlkaissi & McFarlane, 2023; Anghelescu et al., 2023; Biswas et al., 2023; Zohouri et al., 2024). \n\nResearchers and reviewers  \n\nCollaborative research: Conducting collaborative research using GAI tools can make information more \naccessible  and  integrate  diverse  perspectives.  This  practice  leads  to  more  innovative  and  comprehensive \nresearch outcomes. In addition, these tools facilitate global collaboration, allowing researchers from different \ndisciplines  and  locations  to  contribute  to  and  build  upon  each  other\u2019s  work  thereby  accelerating  scientific \nprogress and promoting responsible and ethical use (Abuyaman, 2023; Alkaissi & McFarlane, 2023; Bond et \nal., 2024; Dergaa et al., 2023; Jarrah et al., 2023; Mollaki, 2024; Semrl et al., 2023; Zohouri et al., 2024). \n\nEthical standards and verification: Researchers are required to clearly state the extent and nature of \nGAI involvement in their studies. This ensures transparency and academic integrity which helps maintain the \naccuracy  and  reliability  of  academic  research.  In  addition,  adhering  to  ethical  research  standards  involves \nverifying AI-generated content for accuracy and potential biases in GAI output (Alkaissi & McFarlane, 2023; \nPraveen & Vajrobol, 2023). In the review process, while GAI tools offer benefits, they are not ready to replace \nreviewers due to their limited capabilities in this task. Reviewers must use these tools as supplementary aids \nand verify their output for potential inaccuracies to ensure the integrity of the review process (Biswas et al., \n2023; Checco et al., 2021; Saad et al., 2024). \n\nAI  skill  development:  While  academic  institutions  are  requested  to  provide  training  to  enhance \nresearchers\u2019 AI skills, it is also essential for researchers and reviewers to proactively develop these skills on \ntheir  own.  Researchers  and  reviewers  need  to  enhance  their  AI  literacy  and  prompt  engineering  skills  to \neffectively utilize these tools in academic research. This is crucial because understanding the limitations of \nGAI and the importance of precise prompt input  can significantly  improve the  quality and  reliability  of the \noutput (Abdelhafiz et al., 2024; Khlaif et al., 2023; Zheng et al., 2023). \n\nIMPLICATIONS  \n\nThis review contributes to the knowledge of GAI tools\u2019 integration into academic research workflow and \npeer  review.  The  review  is  one  of  very  few  current  works  in  this  specific  area  of  research.  It  provides  a \n\n14 / 20 \n\nOnline Journal of Communication and Media Technologies, 15(1), e202502 \n\n \n \n\fOnline Journal of Communication and Media Technologies, 2025 \n\ncomprehensive view of academic research workflow tasks that are being automated using GAI tools including \nthe  reviewing  process.  In  addition,  detailed  and  structured  concerns  and  limitations  were  uncovered. \nMoreover, measures to use GAI tools effectively and ethically were analyzed and organized. These findings \nwill  allow  for  a  broader  understanding and  provide  further  directions  for  future  research.  In  addition,  the \nfindings will help various stakeholders in different sectors including researchers, reviewers, policymakers, and \ndevelopers  take  into  consideration  their  roles  and  responsibilities  to  overcome  possible  drawbacks  and \nenhance academic research productivity and quality.  \n\nCONCLUSION \n\nThis  study  presented  systematic  literature  on  applications  of  GAI  tools  in  various  tasks  of  academic \nresearch workflow and peer review process. The study followed the PRISMA protocol to examine 44 journal \nand  conference  articles  from  three  databases,  \u201cScopus\u201d,  \u201cWeb  of  Science\u201d,  and  \u201cPubMed\u201d  The  findings \ndescribed  the  academic  research  and  peer  review  tasks  that  are  currently  experimented  with  GAI  tools, \nincluding  potential  benefits, limitations,  and  concerns  posed  by  researchers\u2019  experiments.  In  addition,  this \nstudy identified measures and recommendations to use these tools better and overcome their limitations.  \n\nLimitations  \n\nThis review exhibits a few limitations. Primarily, due to the evolving nature of GAI tools some literature, \nalthough published recently might not encompass all the new features of this technology. Additionally, the \nstudy focused only on conference and journal articles from three databases, other databases might provide \nmore studies targeting other stages of Academic research in which GAI tools are being examined. Moreover, \nthe study concentrated on Generative text AI tools and did not consider other forms of AI applications that \ncan be utilized in academic research and peer review processes.  \n\nFuture Research  \n\nWith the release of enhanced versions of GAI tools, there is growing potential to significantly impact how \nthey  might  shape the  creation,  revision,  and  dissemination  of  academic  content.  Future  investigations  are \nrequired to explore GAI tools\u2019 applications, particularly AI research assistants, in different stages of research \nand peer review and different disciplines.  \n\nAdditionally, the debate over integrating GAI tools necessitates further research to explore researchers\u2019 \nperceptions  and  attitudes  toward  this  technology.  This  research  should  measure  adoption  levels  within \nspecific demographics, both at individual and organizational levels. Such insights will inform the development \nof tailored policies that address the unique requirements and concerns of each academic institution. \n\nAuthor contributions: HAS, MABA, RBI, & JBM: conception and design; HAS & MABA: material preparation and data \ncollection and analysis; HAS: writing the first draft. Chinna2, Saranya T. S.3 \n1Dr., Professor, School of Commerce & Management, Mohan Babu University, Tirupati, India, ORCID: \nhttps://orcid.org/0000-0002-2670-2858 \n2School of Commerce and Management, Mohan Babu University, Tirupati, India \n3Dr., Associate Professor, Head of the Institute, Amity Institute of Behavioural Health and Allied Sciences, \nAmity University, Bengaluru, India, ORCID: https://orcid.org/0000-0001-7240-4782 \n\nCitation: \nGupta,  S.  K.,  Sravanthi,  K.  D., \nHanitha,  V.,  Kalanjali,  R.  G., \nReddy,  V.  B.,  Prathyusha,  A., \nChinna, V., & T. S., S. (2026). AI-\nBased  HR  Practices  and  Talent \nEffectiveness. \nManagement \nPublic  Administration  and  Law \nReview, \n15\u201329. \n(2(26), \nhttps://doi.org/10.36690/2674-\n5216-2026-2-15-29 \n\nReceived: April 23, 2026 \nApproved: May 26, 2026 \nPublished: June 30, 2026 \n\nThis  article  is  an  open  access \narticle distributed under the terms \nand  conditions  of  the  Creative \nCommons  Attribution  (CC  BY-\nNC 4.0) license \n\nefficiency, \n\nemployee  performance, \n\nAbstract.  Artificial  intelligence  is  increasingly  transforming \nhuman  resource  management  by  automating  routine  processes, \nimproving  data-driven  decision-making  and  supporting  strategic \ntalent management. AI-based HR practices, including recruitment \nautomation,  HR  chatbots,  predictive  analytics  and  AI-powered \nperformance systems, are becoming important tools for improving \nrecruitment \nemployee \ndevelopment and retention. At the same time, the adoption of AI in \nHR creates challenges related to data privacy, algorithmic bias, \ntransparency,  implementation  costs  and  employee  trust.  The \nobjective of this study is to examine the influence of AI-based HR \npractices  on  talent  management  effectiveness  in  organisations, \nwith  particular  attention  to  recruitment,  employee  performance, \nemployee development and retention outcomes. The study applies \na quantitative research approach based on primary and secondary \ndata.  Primary  data  were  collected \nthrough  a  structured \nquestionnaire  distributed  among  100  employees  and  HR \nprofessionals  from  different  organisations.  A  five-point  Likert \nscale  was  used  to  measure  respondents\u2019  perceptions.  The  study \nadopted a descriptive research design and convenience sampling \ntechnique. Structural Equation Modeling was used to analyse the \nrelationship  between  AI-based  HR  practices  as  the  independent \nvariable  and  talent  management  effectiveness  as  the  dependent \nvariable. The findings indicate that AI-based HR practices have a \npositive  influence  on  talent  management  effectiveness.  The  SEM \nresults  show  strong  positive  relationships  between  AI-based  HR \npractices  and  automation,  predictive  analytics  and  AI-powered \nsystems.  The  results  also  demonstrate  that  AI  contributes  to \nrecruitment  efficiency,  employee  performance  and  employee \ndevelopment,  with  employee  development  showing  the  strongest \ncontribution among the outcome variables. AI-based HR practices \nare an important strategic instrument for modern organisations. \nThey  improve  HR  efficiency,  support  evidence-based  decision-\nmaking  and  strengthen  talent  management  outcomes.  Further \nresearch  should  examine  larger  and  more  diverse  samples, \ncompare industries, analyse ethical risks, assess employee trust in \nAI-based HR decisions and investigate the long-term impact of AI \non jobs, organisational culture and workforce development. \nKeywords: artificial intelligence; AI-based HR practices; human \nrecruitment \nresource  management; \nautomation;  HR  analytics;  predictive  analytics;  employee \nperformance;  employee  development;  employee \nretention; \nstructural equation modeling; digital HR transformation. \nJEL Classification: M12; M15; M51; M53; O33; J24. \nFormulas: 0; fig.: 6; tabl.: 2; bibl.: 60 \n\ntalent  management; \n\n15 \n\n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n\fIssue 2 (26), 2026  \ne-ISSN 2674-5216   \n\nPublic Administration and Law Review \n  print-ISSN 2733-211X \n\nIntroduction. The modern commercial landscape is advancing rapidly. \nTechnology is constantly changing, digital disruption challenges the status \nquo,  and  competition  is  not  only  local  but  also  global.  Companies  are \nincreasingly knowledge-driven, and employees are no longer viewed simply \nas  workers.  They  are  among  the  most  valuable  strategic  resources  of  an \norganisation. For this reason, managing talent is not merely an ordinary HR \nresponsibility.  It  is  one  of  the  ways  in  which  organisations  maintain \ncompetitive advantage. \n\nArtificial Intelligence has transformed the landscape of human resource \nmanagement.  It  is  not  only  about  automating  paperwork  or  screening \nresumes.  AI  is  now  embedded  in  how  organisations  identify  candidates, \nsupport  employee  development,  maintain  motivation  and  ensure  retention \n(Maghsoudi et al., 2023). This transformation is changing how organisations \nunderstand talent management and how they design workforce strategies. \n\nArtificial Intelligence refers to computer systems performing tasks that \nwere  previously  assumed to  require  human intelligence,  such as  decision-\nmaking,  problem-solving  and learning  from  previous  experience  (What  Is \nArtificial  Intelligence?,  2020).  In  HR,  AI  appears  in  the  form  of  machine \nlearning systems, natural language processing, predictive modelling, robotic \nprocess automation, conversational agents and intelligent data tools (Garem, \n2026). These systems help HR teams reduce repetitive work, analyse large \ndatasets and make decisions that are more aligned with organisational needs \n(Chukwuka & Dibie, 2024). \nhuman \n\nprimarily \nadministrative  (Lazar,  2020).  Recruiters  manually  reviewed  resumes, \nperformance evaluations often relied on intuition, and employee engagement \nprogrammes  were  rarely  personalised  (Sinclair,  2023).  This  paradigm  has \nchanged.  Technological  transformation  has  moved  HR  beyond  its  former \nadministrative niche and into strategic organisational discussions (Parry & \nBattista,  2019).  AI  technologies  now  help  companies  identify  skill  gaps, \npredict  employee  turnover,  adapt  development  plans  and  align  talent \nstrategies with long-term organisational goals (Zafar, 2023). \n\nresource  management  was \n\nTraditionally, \n\nTalent  management  includes  the  processes  of  attracting,  selecting, \ntraining,  motivating  and  retaining  employees  (Gordana  &  Ili\u0107,  2020).  It \nincludes hiring, onboarding, employee training, performance management, \nsuccession planning, employee engagement and retention (Wandhe, 2025). \nIts primary goal is clear: to place people with the right skills in the right roles \nat the right time (Petkoski & \u0408\u0430\u043a\u043e\u0432\u043b\u0435\u0432, 2022). Effective talent management \nenables  organisations  to  improve  productivity,  strengthen  innovation  and \nrespond to a competitive job market. \n\nAI has changed the way organisations recruit and develop talent. It can \nreview  resumes,  match  skills,  communicate  with  candidates  and  support \nrecruitment  decisions  automatically  (Farhan  &  Hatem,  2024).  Predictive \nanalytics can analyse historical recruitment and performance data to identify \n\n16 \n\n \n \n \n\fIssue 2 (26), 2026  \ne-ISSN 2674-5216   \n\nPublic Administration and Law Review \n  print-ISSN 2733-211X \n\nsuitable candidates (Pessach et al., 2020). These tools allow organisations to \nrecruit faster, reduce human bias and improve the quality of hiring decisions \n(Oman et al., 2024). AI-powered systems can also support more inclusive \nhiring by reaching a wider and more diverse pool of candidates (\u201cCultivating \nSuccess,\u201d 2024). \n\nAI  is  also  transforming  employee  development  and  performance \nevaluation.  Advanced  learning  systems  can  recommend  training  based  on \nemployee  performance  and  career  goals  (Nikitina  et  al.,  2018).  Managers \nreceive  real-time  insights  through  AI  analytics,  allowing  them  to  provide \ntimely support and targeted feedback (Obiki-Osafiele et al., 2024). AI can \nalso identify high-potential employees and support succession planning for \nleadership roles by aligning individual capabilities with organisational needs \n(Natarajan & Dhinakaran, 2024). \n\nEmployee engagement and retention are also strongly connected with \nAI-based  HR  systems.  Machine  learning  tools  can  analyse  surveys, \ncommunication  patterns  and  employee  data  to  identify  early  signs  of \ndisengagement  (Evseeva  et  al.,  2021).  Predictive  models  can  identify \nemployees who may be considering leaving the organisation and give HR \nmanagers  an  opportunity  to  intervene  (Tang  et  al.,  2025).  As  a  result, \norganisations can design better retention strategies and improve employee \nsatisfaction (Hinge et al., 2023). \n\nHowever,  the  adoption  of  AI  in  HR  is  not  without  challenges.  Data \nprotection and privacy must be addressed because AI systems require large \nvolumes of employee data (Kaur & Gandolfi, 2023). There are also concerns \nabout  fairness,  algorithmic  bias  and  transparency,  especially  when \nautomated  decisions  affect  recruitment,  promotion  or  performance \nevaluation (Cabiddu et al., 2022). Overreliance on technology may make HR \nappear impersonal and may negatively affect trust and organisational culture \n(Berg et al., 2020). Moreover, AI implementation can be costly, and not all \norganisations  have  the  technical  skills  required  to  use  these  systems \neffectively. \n\nLiterature  review.  Artificial  Intelligence  has  become  an  important \ntechnological  advancement  that  is  transforming  modern  HRM  practices. \nOrganisations increasingly use AI tools to automate HR functions such as \nrecruitment, employee engagement, performance evaluation and workforce \nplanning  (Sundari  et  al.,  2024).  AI  technologies  allow  HR  managers  to \nanalyse large volumes of employee data and generate insights that support \nbetter decision-making (Nyathani, 2023). \n\nStrohmeier  and  Piazza  (2015),  in  their  conceptual  exploration  of \nartificial  intelligence  techniques  in  HRM,  analysed  how  AI,  including \nmachine learning, expert systems and data mining, can transform essential \nHR  activities  such  as  hiring,  employee  selection,  training  and  employee \nperformance tracking. They concluded that AI assists HR teams in making \nmore informed decisions and improving efficiency by analysing employee \n\n17 \n\n \n \n \n\fIssue 2 (26), 2026  \ne-ISSN 2674-5216   \n\nPublic Administration and Law Review \n  print-ISSN 2733-211X \n\ndata,  detecting  patterns  and  supporting  planning.  However,  they  also \nhighlighted  concerns  regarding  ethics,  data  protection  and  the  need  for \ncontinuous human oversight. \n\nVotto et al. (2021), in a systematic literature review on AI in tactical \nhuman  resource  management,  examined  how  AI  is  used  in  recruitment, \nperformance reviews, workforce training, workplace conduct management \nand compensation. Their findings show that AI is increasingly integrated into \nHuman  Resource  Information  Systems  and  helps  HR  teams  work  more \nefficiently  through  data-based  decision-making.  At  the  same  time,  they \nobserved that most AI applications are concentrated in technical and data-\nheavy  tasks,  while  more  human-centred  managerial  decisions  remain \ninsufficiently explored. \n\nB\u00f6hmer  and  Schinnenburg  (2023)  analysed  AI-driven  HRM  through \nthe  Resource-Based  View  and  examined  how  AI-enabled  HRM  can  build \norganisational capabilities. They identified four important areas of difficulty: \nrole structuring, openness, performance review and organisational data use. \nTheir  research  suggests  that  AI  can  improve  strategic  HRM  and \norganisational  growth,  but  its  benefits  depend  on  how  leaders  manage \ntransparency, data and human involvement. \n\nTinguely et al. (2023) examined the design of HRM systems in the age \nof  AI.  Their  analysis  shows  that  AI  changes  established  HR  practices  by \nenabling  data-based  decisions,  automation  of  repetitive  tasks  and  better \ntalent management. However, they emphasise that organisations should not \nallow  machines  to  take  full  control.  The  best  HR  solutions  combine  AI \ntechnologies with human judgement to ensure fairness and transparency. \n\nBharadwaj  (2024)  provided  a  comprehensive  overview  of  AI \nintegration  in  HRM  and  argued  that  AI  is  reshaping  recruitment,  talent \nidentification,  employee  engagement,  staff  training  and  performance \nmeasurement.  AI  enables  organisations  to  work  faster  and  make  more \naccurate decisions. However, ethical issues, data privacy and transparency \nremain significant challenges. \n\nTalent management refers to the process of attracting, developing and \nretaining skilled employees (Karalar & Atay, 2020; Singh, 2021). Effective \ntalent management ensures that employees with the right skills are placed in \nappropriate roles to achieve organisational goals (Attar et al., 2025; Touwely \net al., 2023). AI-based HR practices support talent management by helping \norganisations identify talented employees, monitor performance and design \npersonalised training programmes (Alsaif & Aksoy, 2023; Suneetha et al., \n2024). These tools also help HR managers understand employee behaviour \nand  engagement  levels,  which  supports  retention  strategies  (Sabil  et  al., \n2023; Vishwanath, 2023). \n\nRecruitment is one of the major areas where AI technologies are widely \nused (Rathore, 2023; Wa\u017cna, 2024). AI-powered recruitment systems help \norganisations analyse resumes, shortlist candidates and schedule interviews \n\n18 \n\n \n \n \n\fIssue 2 (26), 2026  \ne-ISSN 2674-5216   \n\nPublic Administration and Law Review \n  print-ISSN 2733-211X \n\nautomatically  (Farhan  &  Hatem,  2024;  Khan  et  al.,  2024).  This  reduces \nrecruitment time and improves the accuracy of candidate selection (Biliavska \net al., 2022; N\u0103stase et al., 2024). \n\nAI is also useful in employee development. AI-based learning platforms \nanalyse employee performance and recommend training programmes based \non  skill  requirements  and  career  development  needs  (Bura  et  al.,  2025; \nFarhan & Hatem, 2024). These systems help organisations design effective \ndevelopment  programmes  that  improve  employee  capabilities  and  career \ngrowth (K, 2026; Tusquellas et al., 2024). \n\nPrevious  studies  have  focused  on  HR  analytics  and  digital  HR \ntechnologies in  improving  organisational  performance (Kiran  et  al.,  2023; \nTessema et al., 2025). However, limited studies have specifically examined \nthe  role  of  AI-based  HR  practices  in  improving  talent  management \neffectiveness. Most research focuses on analytics tools for decision-making \nrather  than  understanding  how  AI  influences  recruitment  efficiency, \nemployee  development  and  employee  retention.  Therefore,  this  study \naddresses this gap by examining how AI-based HR practices contribute to \ntalent management effectiveness in organisations. \n\nAims. The aim of this study is to examine how AI-based HR practices \n\ninfluence talent management effectiveness in organisations. \n\nThe objectives of the study are: \n\n1. To  analyse  the  role  of  AI-based  HR  practices  in  recruitment  and  talent \n\nacquisition. \n\n2. To  examine  the  influence  of  AI  tools  on  employee  performance  and \n\ndevelopment. \n\n3. To  determine  the  relationship  between  AI-based  HR  practices  and \n\nemployee retention. \n\n4. To  evaluate  the  overall  impact  of  AI-based  HR  practices  on  talent \n\nmanagement effectiveness. \n\n5. To identify the benefits and challenges of AI adoption in HRM. \n6. To provide practical insights for organisations seeking to integrate AI into \n\ntalent management systems. \n\nThe hypotheses of the study are formulated as follows: \n\n\u2212  H1:  AI-based  HR  practices  positively  influence  recruitment  and  talent \n\nacquisition. \n\n\u2212  H2:  AI-based  HR  practices  positively  influence  employee  performance \n\nand development. \n\n\u2212  H3: AI-based HR practices significantly improve employee retention. \n\u2212  H4:  AI-based  HR  practices  significantly  improve  talent  management \n\neffectiveness. \n\nMethodology. This study is based on both primary and secondary data \nsources.  Primary  data  were  collected  through  a  structured  questionnaire \ndistributed  among  employees  and  HR  professionals  working  in  different \n\n19 \n\n \n \n \n\fIssue 2 (26), 2026  \ne-ISSN 2674-5216   \n\nPublic Administration and Law Review \n  print-ISSN 2733-211X \n\norganisations.  Secondary  data  were  collected  from  research  articles, \nacademic  journals,  books  and  credible  online  sources  related  to  Artificial \nIntelligence and Human Resource Management. \n\nA pragmatist research philosophy was adopted. Pragmatism focuses on \npractical solutions and real-world applications. This philosophy allows the \nstudy  to  understand  how  AI  technologies  are  practically  applied  in  HR \npractices and how they influence talent management in organisations. \n\nA quantitative research approach was used. Data were collected through \nstructured  questionnaires  and  analysed  using  statistical  techniques  to \nunderstand  the  relationship  between  AI-based  HR  practices  and  talent \nmanagement effectiveness. \n\nThe study adopted a descriptive research design. Descriptive research \nhelps  transform  collected  data  into  meaningful  information  that  can  be \ninterpreted and analysed (Alfred, 2020). The sampling technique used in this \nstudy was convenience sampling. The sample consisted of 100 respondents, \nincluding  employees  and  HR  professionals  who  are  familiar  with  HR \npractices and AI-based HR systems. \n\nThe independent variable of the study is AI-based HR practices, while \nthe  dependent  variable  is  talent  management  effectiveness.  AI-based  HR \npractices include tools such as automated recruitment systems, AI chatbots, \nHR  analytics  tools  and  AI-based  performance  management  systems \n(Maghsoudi  et  al.,  2023).  Talent  management  effectiveness  refers  to  an \norganisation\u2019s  ability  to  attract,  develop,  engage  and  retain  talented \nemployees (\u0145ikadimovs & Ivanchenko, 2020). \n\nA  five-point  Likert  scale  ranging  from  Strongly  Disagree  (1)  to \n\nStrongly Agree (5) was used to measure respondents\u2019 perceptions. \n\nThe  research  model  explains  the  relationship  between  AI-based  HR \npractices and talent management effectiveness. In this study, AI-based HR \npractices are considered the independent variable, while talent management \neffectiveness is considered the dependent variable. The model examines how \nAI tools such as recruitment automation, AI chatbots, HR analytics and AI-\nbased  performance  systems  influence  recruitment  efficiency,  employee \nperformance, employee retention and employee development. \n\nResults.  The  demographic  profile  of  respondents  shows  that  the \nmajority  of  respondents  were  male, representing  64%  of  the  total sample, \nwhile female respondents represented 36%. With respect to age distribution, \nmost respondents were below 30 years old, representing 42% of the sample. \nRespondents aged 31 to 40 years represented 36%, while respondents aged \n41 to 50 years represented 22%. There were no respondents above 50 years \nold. \n\n20 \n\n \n \n \n \n\fIssue 2 (26), 2026  \ne-ISSN 2674-5216   \n\nPublic Administration and Law Review \n  print-ISSN 2733-211X \n\nSource: developed by the authors based on the research model of the study. \n\nFigure 1. Structural model of the study \n\nIn terms of educational qualification, the majority of respondents were \ngraduates, representing 48% of the sample. Postgraduates represented 14%, \ndiploma holders represented 10%, and respondents with other qualifications \nrepresented  28%.  Regarding  work  experience,  most  respondents  had  less \nthan five years of experience, representing 56% of the sample. Respondents \nwith  5 to  10  years  of  experience  represented  28%,  while those  with more \nthan 10 years of experience represented 16%. \n\nCategory \n\nTable 1. This profile \nis relevant to the study because younger and early-career employees may be \nmore familiar with digital HR systems and AI-based tools. \n\nindicate \n\nStructural  Equation  Modeling  analysis.  Structural  Equation \nModeling  was  used  to  analyse  the  relationship  between  variables.  In  this \nstudy, SEM was used to examine the impact of AI-based HR practices on \ntalent  management  effectiveness.  SEM  helps  to  understand  how  different \nfactors are interconnected and how they influence organisational outcomes. \nThe  values  shown  in  the  SEM  model  represent  standardised  path \ncoefficients.  These  coefficients  indicate  the  strength  and  direction  of  the \nrelationship between variables. A higher value closer to 1 indicates a stronger \npositive relationship. \n\nThe  relationship  between  AI-based  HR  practices  and  automation  is \n0.80, indicating a strong positive influence. The path coefficient between AI-\nbased  HR  practices  and  predictive  analytics  is  0.85,  which  shows  a  very \nstrong impact. Similarly, the value of 0.84 between AI-based HR practices \nand AI-powered systems indicates a strong relationship. \n\n22 \n\n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n\fIssue 2 (26), 2026  \ne-ISSN 2674-5216   \n\nPublic Administration and Law Review \n  print-ISSN 2733-211X \n\nFigure 6. Structural Equation Model of AI-based HR practices on \ntalent effectiveness \n\nSource: developed by the authors based on SEM results from primary survey data. \n\nThe relationship between automation and talent effectiveness is 0.77, \nshowing a moderate to strong impact. The coefficient of 0.84 between AI-\npowered systems and talent effectiveness indicates a strong influence. The \nvalue  of  0.81  for  employee  performance  suggests  a  strong  contribution  to \ntalent effectiveness, while the coefficient of 0.86 for employee development \nindicates the highest impact among the outcome variables. \n\nTable 2. This \ntalent \n\nthat  AI-based  HR  practices  significantly \n\nimprove \n\nindicates \nmanagement effectiveness in organisations. \n\n23 \n\n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n\fIssue 2 (26), 2026  \ne-ISSN 2674-5216   \n\nPublic Administration and Law Review \n  print-ISSN 2733-211X \n\nDiscussion. The findings indicate that AI-based HR practices play an \nimportant role in improving talent management within organisations. Many \norganisations  are  adopting  AI  technologies  to  improve  recruitment \nefficiency,  employee  performance  evaluation  and  workforce  planning \n(Fenwick et al., 2024). AI tools help HR managers analyse employee data \nand identify potential talent within the organisation (Suneetha et al., 2024). \ntraining \ntechnologies  also  help  organisations  design  better \nThese \nprogrammes and improve employee engagement (Baki et al., 2023). \n\nThe results support the first hypothesis, which proposed that AI-based \nHR  practices  positively  influence  recruitment  and  talent  acquisition.  AI \nrecruitment  systems  reduce  manual  workload,  improve  resume  screening \nand  allow  HR  professionals  to  identify  suitable  candidates  more  quickly \n(Farhan & Hatem, 2024; Rathore, 2023). This supports the view that AI can \nimprove recruitment efficiency and candidate selection accuracy. \n\nThe findings also support the second hypothesis, which proposed that \nAI-based  HR  practices  positively  influence  employee  performance  and \ndevelopment. AI-based performance systems and learning platforms allow \norganisations to monitor employee progress, identify skill gaps and provide \npersonalised  development  recommendations  (Alsaif  &  Aksoy,  2023; \nTusquellas  et  al.,  2024).  The  SEM  coefficient  for  employee  development \nwas the highest among the outcome variables, which suggests that AI may \nhave particularly strong value in learning and career development. \n\nThe  third  hypothesis,  which  proposed  that  AI-based  HR  practices \nimprove employee retention, is also supported conceptually by the literature \nand the study model. Predictive analytics can identify employees who may \nbe at risk of leaving and help HR managers design retention strategies (Tang \net  al.,  2025).  By  analysing  engagement  data,  organisations  can  respond \nearlier  to  employee  dissatisfaction  and  improve  workplace  experience \n(Vishwanath, 2023). \n\nThe  fourth  hypothesis,  which  proposed  that  AI-based  HR  practices \nsignificantly improve talent management effectiveness, is supported by the \noverall  SEM  results.  Positive  path  coefficients  indicate  that  AI-based  HR \npractices  contribute  to  recruitment  efficiency,  employee  performance, \nemployee development and retention. These results confirm that AI-based \nHR  systems  have  become  an important  strategic tool  for  improving  talent \nmanagement and organisational performance. \n\nHowever,  AI  adoption  in  HR  also  creates  several  challenges.  Data \nprivacy is a major concern because AI systems require access to employee \nand  candidate  data  (Kaur  &  Gandolfi,  2023).  Algorithmic  bias  is  another \nsignificant  issue  because  biased  data  or  poorly  designed  algorithms  may \nproduce  unfair  outcomes  in  hiring,  promotion  or  performance  evaluation \n(Cabiddu et al., 2022). Employee resistance may also occur when workers \nfear  job  loss,  mistrust  automated  decisions  or  perceive  AI  systems  as \nimpersonal (Berg et al., 2020). \n\n24 \n\n \n \n \n\fIssue 2 (26), 2026  \ne-ISSN 2674-5216   \n\nPublic Administration and Law Review \n  print-ISSN 2733-211X \n\nTherefore,  organisations  should  combine  AI  tools  with  human \noversight. AI can improve HR efficiency and decision-making, but it should \nnot  replace  human  judgement  in  sensitive  personnel  decisions.  Ethical  AI \nuse in HR requires transparency, fairness, accountability and employee trust. \nConclusions. This study examined the role of AI-based HR practices \nin improving talent management effectiveness. The findings indicate that the \nadoption  of  AI  technologies  in  HR  processes  can  enhance  recruitment \nefficiency,  employee  engagement,  employee  performance,  employee \ndevelopment and workforce planning. \n\nin \nAI-based  HR  practices  enable  data-driven  decision-making \nworkforce management, helping organisations identify and analyse talented \nindividuals more effectively (Arora & Babu, 2025). The SEM results show \nthat  AI-based  HR  practices  have  strong  positive  relationships  with \nautomation,  predictive  analytics  and  AI-powered  systems.  These  tools \ncontribute to talent effectiveness, especially through employee development, \nemployee performance and recruitment efficiency. \n\nThe  study  concludes  that  AI-based  HR  practices  have  become  an \nessential  component  of modern  human  resource  management (Fenwick  et \nal.,  2024).  Organisations that invest  in  AI technologies  are more likely  to \nimprove  workforce  efficiency  and  maintain  competitiveness  in  today\u2019s \ndigital business environment (Adigwe et al., 2024). \n\nto  ethics,  privacy, \n\nAt  the  same  time,  the  successful  use  of  AI  in  HR  requires  careful \nattention \ntransparency  and  human  oversight. \nOrganisations should not rely on AI as a fully autonomous decision-maker. \nInstead,  AI  should  be  used  as  a  support  tool  that  strengthens  HR \nprofessionals\u2019 ability to make informed, fair and strategic decisions. \n\nThe  study  has  some  limitations.  The  sample  size  was  limited  to  100 \nrespondents due to time constraints, so the results may not represent all types \nof  organisations.  Another  limitation  is  that  most  data  were  based  on \nrespondents\u2019 personal opinions, which may introduce subjective bias. \n\nFuture research should use larger and more diverse samples, compare \norganisations  across  sectors  and  examine  how  AI-based  HR  practices \ninfluence  long-term  employee  outcomes.  Researchers  should  also  study \nissues  such  as  data  privacy,  algorithmic  bias,  employee  trust,  job \ndisplacement concerns and the impact of AI on organisational culture. \n\nConflict  of  interest.  The  authors  declare  that  the  research  was \nconducted in the absence of any commercial or financial relationships that \ncould be construed as a potential conflict of interest. \n\nFunding.  The  authors  declare that  no financial  support  was  received \n\nfor the research, authorship, and/or publication of this article. \n\nEthics  statement. Unleashing AI potential in human resource management: A case study of corporate \nsector  in  Karachi.  Global  Journal  for  Management  and  Administrative  Sciences,  4(1),  65. \nhttps://doi.org/10.46568/gjmas.v4i1.182 \n\n29 \n\n \n \n \n \n\f",
outputs/journal_corpus_content.json:17301:  "fulltext": "Journal of Ecohumanism \n2024 \nVolume: 3, No: 8, pp. 13868 \u2013 13875 \nISSN: 2752-6798 (Print) | ISSN 2752-6801 (Online) \nhttps://ecohumanism.co.uk/joe/ecohumanism  \nDOI: https://doi.org/10.62754/joe.v3i8.6513  \n\nArtificial Intelligence in Dental Radiology: Review of  the Impacts on \nDiagnostic Accuracy and Nursing Practices  \nMisfer Hussein Misfer Qahman1, Raiyyah Abdulhdi Almowallad2, Raniah Mohammed Rashed \nAlluqmani3, Rana Mohammed Rashed Alluqmani4, Rehab Saud  Alghamdi5, Bandar Abdulaziz \nTharban Al-Otaibi6, Abdullah Hussain Hassan Alshahrani7, Samer Mohammed Alghamdi8 \n\nAbstract  \n\nThe use of artificial intelligence (AI) in dental radiography is revolutionizing  diagnostic methodologies and nursing  responsibilities \nwithin the healthcare domain. As AI technologies progress, comprehending their influence on diagnostic precision and nursing practices \nis crucial for future education and clinical implementation.A thorough literature analysis was performed, using papers from databases \nincluding  PubMed,  Scopus,  and  Google  Scholar  till  2023.  The  review  concentrated  on  assessing  the  knowledge,  attitudes,  and \ncompetencies of healthcare, dental, and nursing students concerning AI applications in dental radiology.The findings indicated that most \nof studies exhibited a moderate to strong comprehension of AI, accompanied by notable deficiencies in practical knowledge and skills. \nAlthough  65%  exhibited  favorable  attitudes  towards  AI  implementation  in  clinical  environments,  apprehensions  regarding \ndehumanization  and  job  displacement  were  widespread,  particularly  among  students  from  disadvantaged  areas.  The  majority  of \nparticipants  supported  the  incorporation  of  AI  education  into  healthcare  courses  to  improve  comprehension  and  practical \nimplementation.AI offers both advantages and obstacles in dental radiology, requiring a targeted educational strategy to prepare future \nhealthcare practitioners with essential competencies. A curriculum that prioritizes practical applications, ethical considerations, and the \ncollaborative function of AI in augmenting human capabilities is essential. Longitudinal studies are advised to track alterations in \nknowledge and attitudes following graduation and to evaluate the efficacy of AI training in enhancing clinical outcomes. \n\nKeywords: Artificial Intelligence, Dental Radiology, Nursing Education, Diagnostic Accuracy, Healthcare Training. \n\nIntroduction \n\nThe phrase \"artificial intelligence (AI)\" became prevalent around 70 years ago to describe the utilization of  \ncomputers to replicate human thinking. The first use of  AI occurred in mathematics in 1956 when it was \nemployed for theorem proving [1,2]. The integration of  AI in healthcare was a lengthy process that started \nwith the creation of  a computer program that advised physicians on suitable antibiotic medication [3]. \n\nArtificial intelligence is a prominent subject in the front of  technological progress and has the capacity to \nsubstantially impact the healthcare sector. The term AI denotes a scientific and technical field focused on \ncreating computer systems that demonstrate intelligent behavior and can comprehend and replicate human \ncognitive  processes  [4-6].  Recent  breakthroughs  in  computer  and  bioinformatics  innovations  have \nfacilitated  the  integration  of   AI  technologies,  including  machine  learning  as  well  as  deep  learning,  into \nmedical computer systems [7- 9]. Artificial intelligence has been thoroughly incorporated into systems for \ndecision  support  (DSSs)  within  data-intensive  medical  fields  such  as  pathology,  radiology  as  well  as \nophthalmology [10]. \n\nNumerous  specialists  have  articulated  their  perspectives  on  the  prospects  of   radiology  considering  the \nadvent of  AI [11, 12]. Radiological organizations have issued white papers advocating their perspectives \n\n1 Ksa, Ministry of Health, Irada Complex for Mental Health \n2 Ksa, Ministry of Health, King Fahad General Hospital. \n3 Ksa, Ministry of Health, Primary Health Care Alsafa 1. \n4 Ksa, Ministry of Health, Primary Health Care Alrehab. \n\n5 Ksa, Ministry of Health, Primary Health Care Alsafa 1 \n\n6 Ksa, Ministry of Health, Al-Dawadmi General Hospital Health Assistant \n\n7 Ksa, Ministry of Health, Wadi Bin Hashbal Sector  Khyber South Health Center \n\n8 Ksa, Ministry of Health, Dental Department, Al-Lith General Hospital, Jeddah \n\n13868 \n\n \n \n \n \n \n                                                      \n\fJournal of Ecohumanism \n2024 \nVolume: 3, No: 8, pp. 13868 \u2013 13875 \nISSN: 2752-6798 (Print) | ISSN 2752-6801 (Online) \nhttps://ecohumanism.co.uk/joe/ecohumanism  \nDOI: https://doi.org/10.62754/joe.v3i8.6513  \n[13,  14].  Research  has  shown  that  medical  learners  do  not  exhibit  much  apprehension  or  anxiety  over \npotential  replacement  by  AI  in  their  chosen  profession  [15].  Some  students  may  suffer  worry  over  the \npotential displacement by AI, which might deter them from pursuing certain medical specialty [16]. There \nare  both  advantageous  and  disadvantageous  viewpoints  about  the  influence  of   AI  on  everyday  human \nexistence.  Pessimistic  perspectives  indicate  that  AI  might  replace  humans  across  several  industries. \nConversely, optimistic perspectives emphasize that persons aided by AI will possess enhanced possibilities \nto capitalize on future developments [17-23]. This research sought to assess the views, understanding, and \nabilities of  healthcare, dentistry, and nursing students on AI, as well as to collect their perspectives on its \nuse. \n\nMethodology \n\nA  thorough  literature  search  was  conducted  till  2023  to  gather  relevant  papers  from  the  Scopus, \nPubMed/MEDLINE, as well as Google Scholar libraries. \n\nThe Understanding of  AI Concepts and Applications \n\nIn  all,  44  percent  of   learners  demonstrated  a  moderate  to  strong  understanding  of   AI  concepts  and \napplications.  Knowledge  included  theoretical  comprehension  of   AI  techniques,  practical  skills  for \nimplementing AI systems, as well as programming expertise. Nonetheless, the bulk of  pupils have a limited \nunderstanding  of   AI.  This  knowledge  deficiency  indicates  an  immediate  need  to  include  extensive  AI \ninstruction in healthcare curriculum. Research indicates that pupils endorse this concept [24-34]. Curricula \nmust include fundamental ideas such as machine learning as well as neural networks, with practical abilities \nin using AI technologies for applications such as diagnostic imaging analysis. Practical practical learning \nusing real-world case studies may be very successful. A significant hurdle to the use of  AI is the lack of  \nknowledge.  Students  from  rich  countries  exhibited  a  superior  understanding  of   AI  compared  to  their \ncounterparts in underdeveloped ones. This is being demonstrated in another research as well [35]. This \ndisparity  underscores  alarming  worldwide  digital  disparities  in  obtaining  AI  skills  training.  Strategic \ninvestments and capacity-building initiatives are essential for enabling students globally to acquire applied \nAI skills. \n\nDespite their varying levels of  understanding, sixty-five percent of  learners expressed favorable opinions \ntowards  the  use  of   AI  in  educational  and  clinical  settings.  Previous  research  have  also  shown  that  the \nmajority of  healthcare students had a positive mindset regarding AI [19, 36-39]. Students acknowledged the \nprospective  advantages  of   AI  in  augmenting  diagnostic  precision,  boosting  healthcare  accessibility,  and \nalleviating clinical burdens. Conversely, there are unfavorable impressions as well [36, 40, 41]. \n\nAttitudinal  variables  exhibited  significant  variation,  indicating  varying  perspectives  across  student \ngroupings.  Students  from  impoverished  nations  exhibited  more  skepticism,  apprehensive  that  AI  may \ndehumanize  care  or  eliminate  healthcare  employment  opportunities.  Curricula  must  confront  these \nlegitimate  ethical  and  social  issues  by  examining  artificial  intelligence  prejudice,  honesty,  and  effects  on \nhealthcare  professions.  Patient  autonomy  and  confidentiality,  informed  consent,  openness,  equality,  and \nbiases  are  significant  considerations.  Enhancing  attitudinal  metrics  via  more  detailed  subgroups  and \ninvestigating  determinants  of   AI  adoption  will  better  guide  tailored  educational  programs  addressing \nstudents' particular concerns [42]. \n\nStudents  exhibited  excitement  and  confidence  regarding  AI's  potential  in  medicine;  nevertheless,  most \nlacked significant experience and practical skills in using AI technology. The same trend is seen in other \ndisciplines as well [43]. Research conducted by Busch et al. with 387 pharmacy students across 12 countries \nrevealed that 58% of  the participants had favorable views regarding AI in medicine, but 63% indicated a \nlimited general understanding of  AI [44]. Addressing these attitude-knowledge disparities is a significant \nbarrier for AI preparedness. Curricula must not alone provide technical information but also include values, \nethics,  and  social  implications.  Education  should  prioritize  AI  as  a  cooperative  instrument  to  enhance \nhuman  talents  instead  of   substituting  them.  Once  again,  allowing  students  to  actually  experience  the \nadvantages  of   AI  in  care  quality  might  demonstrate  its  capacity  to  augment  labor  rather  than  displace \n\n13869 \n\n \n\fJournal of Ecohumanism \n2024 \nVolume: 3, No: 8, pp. 13868 \u2013 13875 \nISSN: 2752-6798 (Print) | ISSN 2752-6801 (Online) \nhttps://ecohumanism.co.uk/joe/ecohumanism  \nDOI: https://doi.org/10.62754/joe.v3i8.6513  \nemployees.  Moreover,  equal  admission  to  AI  upgrading  is  essential,  especially  for  students  from \nunderprivileged areas who may possess increased apprehensions about the hazards of  AI. \n\nUtilization of  Artificial Intelligence in Dentistry \n\nThe  diagnostic  reasoning  for  a  specific  illness  relies  on  a  clinician's  evaluation  of   signs,  diagnostic  test \noutcomes, and other criteria, which are susceptible to the clinician's flawed recall and cognitive biases. When \ntrained  on  tens  of   thousands  of   instances,  AI  exceeds  the  clinical  expertise  of   even  the  most  skilled \nspecialists [45]. Machine learning methods, such as Support Vector Machines, Artificial Neural Networks, \nRandom Forests, and k-nearest-neighbor methods, were empirically examined for their efficacy in detecting \ncysts, benign malignancies, mouth cancer, as well as lymph node metastases. Utilizing meticulously designed \ncharacteristics  in  cone  beam  computerized  tomography  (CBCT),  SVM  achieved  94%  accuracy  in \ndistinguishing periapical lesions from keratocystic odontogenic cancers [46]. A risk classification system \nusing  brush  sample  as  well  as  cytology  was  developed  employing  machine  learning  methods,  including \nsupport vector machines and random forests. This model used CNN to evaluate the tumor of  cytology \npictures  obtained  from  a  telehealth  system,  demonstrating  significant  sensitivity  in  identifying  oral \ncancerous (93 percent) and high-grade prospective cancerous (73 percent) tumors [47]. Notwithstanding \nthese  commendable  outcomes,  current  AI  models  for  diagnosing  dental  and  maxillofacial  surgery \nconcentrate  just on  a singular  form  of   data,  including radiographic  findings  or  cytopathologic pictures. \nModels that include extensive medical data about an individual are necessary for precise diagnosis. \n\nDeep learning with convolutional neural networks has emerged as the primary artificial intelligence tool for \nautomatic lesion segmentation in cariology as well as endodontic diagnosis. The segmentation procedure \npartitions radiographs or pictures into distinct nonoverlapping parts based on certain criteria, such as pixel \nsimilarity or inherent characteristics, to transform them into a format that facilitates analysis [48]. Utilizing \nan architecture consisting of  encode (U-Net), deep learning split CBCT voxels into categories of  \u201clesion,\u201d \n\u201ctooth framework,\u201d \u201cbone,\u201d \u201ctherapeutic substances,\u201d and \u201cbackground,\u201d attaining findings equivalent to \ndoctors in the diagnosis of  periapical tumors [49]. By concentrating on the binary existence or nonexistence \nof   lesions,  deep  learning  detected  proximate  serious  lesions  from  near-infrared  illumination  pictures, \nachieving  an  area  underneath  the  recipient's  operating  feature  curve  of   0.856  [50,  51].  Volumetric \nassessment in CBCT, subsequent to DL-based segmentation, was shown to be similar to the outcomes \nderived from manually dividing of  periapical tumors [52]. This study does not publish findings like the \nstandard variation of  lesions as well as the junction over Union measure. This omission undermines the \ndependability of  the findings. \n\nPeriodontal illness is a multifaceted inflammatory condition influenced by several causative variables that \noperate  concurrently  and  interactively.  In  the  context  of   periapical  radiographs,  the  CNN  attained \naccuracies of  81.0% and 76.7% in detecting periodontally deficient premolars and molars, respectively [53]. \nNonetheless, owing to the repeatability of  scanning attributes and the visual scope of  periapical images, \nthis  method  is  incapable  of   differentiating  incipient  lesions  or  rendering  an  accurate  identification  of  \nperiodontal illness. Decision Trees (DT) and Support Vector Machines (SVM) effectively classified healthy \nperiodontium, gingivitis, persistent periodontal disease, as well as aggressive periodontitis by including a \npatient's  medical  history,  clinical  data,  and  radiographic  images.  While  the  immunologic  replies  and \nmicrobial diversity associated with the etiology of  periodontitis remain inadequately understood, AI-based \nclassifiers\u2014specifically  a  multilayer  perceptron  neural  networks  (a  type  of   feedforward  artificial  neural \nnetwork) concentrating on leukocytes, interleukins, as well as IgG antibody titers, alongside a support vector \nmachine emphasizing relative bacterial load\u2014demonstrated efficacy in differentiating rough from persistent \nperiodontal disease [54]. AI not only enhances our comprehension of  periodontitis but also facilitates the \nintegration  of   traditional  indications  with  immunological  and  microbiological  data  in  periodontal \ndiagnostics. \n\nClinical  indicators  derived  from  a  patient's  complaints  and  medical  history  are  crucial  for  identifying \ndisorders of  the temporomandibular joint (TMDs). The processing of  natural languages is a technique that \nconverts  human  language  into  organized  programming  language.  A  model  based  on  natural  language \nprocessing  effectively  distinguished  TMD-mimicking  symptoms  from  genuine  TMDs  by  analyzing  the \n\n13870 \n\n \n\fJournal of Ecohumanism \n2024 \nVolume: 3, No: 8, pp. 13868 \u2013 13875 \nISSN: 2752-6798 (Print) | ISSN 2752-6801 (Online) \nhttps://ecohumanism.co.uk/joe/ecohumanism  \nDOI: https://doi.org/10.62754/joe.v3i8.6513  \nfrequency of  word use in the patient's major complaint and the amount of  mouth opening [55]. Artificial \nneural  networks  using  cone-beam  computed  tomography  demonstrated  a  91.2%  concordance  with \nphysician agreement in the classification of  condylar structure [56]. Integrating patients' primary concerns, \nclinical  as  well  as  biochemical  measures,  and  quantitative  radiomic  characteristics  into  training  datasets, \ntogether with the collection of  higher sample sizes, necessitates the development of  a computer-assisted \ndiagnostic system to enhance the accuracy of  TMD diagnoses. \n\nA knowledge-based system has been created to identify clearly identifiable cephalometric landmarks. The \nmethod demonstrated a mean inaccuracy of  just 2.01 mm across all 20 evaluated landmarks [57]. Diagnostic \nmodeling of  cephalometric lateral scans was developed employing machine learning methods, including \nartificial neural networks, support vector machines, random forests, and decision trees, to assess cervical \nvertebral development. Among the evaluated machine learning methods, artificial neural networks (ANN) \nyielded the most favorable outcomes in categorizing cervical vertebral maturation stages, whereas decision \ntrees  (DT)  excelled  in  vertebral  body  form  classification  [58].  In  the  future,  hybrid  methodologies \nintegrating knowledge-based algorithms and machine learning are expected to provide more precise and \naccurate computerized cephalometric analysis. \n\nConcentrating  on  the  proliferation  of   inflammatory  cytokine  genes,  ANN,  SVM,  and  RF  effectively \ndifferentiated lichen planus of  the mouth from various white diseases of  the oral cavity [58]. In the context \nof  identifying steatosis (i.e., abnormal lipid accumulation) in salivary gland parenchyma by ultrasonography, \nartificial neural networks (ANN) outperformed novice radiologists in distinguishing patients with authentic \nSj\u00f6gren\u2019s disease from individuals with xerostomia [59]. \n\nThe  robustness  of   our  analysis  lies  in  the  examination  of   publications  from  three  extensive  databases: \nScopus,  PubMed,  as  well  as  Google  Scholar. Furthermore,  we  utilized  the  model of   random  effects  to \nascertain the reliability of  the findings. Furthermore, our research has several limitations. We included solely \nstudies published in English. Furthermore, the majority of  the studies employed proprietary questionnaires \nto assess participants' knowledge and attitudes regarding artificial intelligence. Ultimately, it is essential to \nnote that insufficient studies were available to derive skill outcomes and conduct a meta-analysis. \n\nProspective Avenues for Study \n\nFuture studies should examine the long-term understanding and behavioral developments of  student\u2019s post-\ngraduation. Longitudinal data monitoring cohorts of  learners in practice might provide critical insights to \ninform ongoing learning and methods for managing change. \n\nSubsequent research should evaluate the longevity of  machine learning abilities development. Can singular \ninstruction provide enduring abilities, or is continuous reinforcement necessary? Comparative analyses of  \nvarious pedagogical methodologies for AI education may reveal optimal practices. Future research must \ncritically assess the connections between AI education and tangible improvements in healthcare procedures \nand patient outcomes. Exhibiting advantages to care quality is the most compelling motivation for curricular \nmodification. \n\nSummary \n\nThe use of  artificial intelligence (AI) in dental radiography is transforming diagnostic methods and nursing \nresponsibilities  in  healthcare.  This  thorough  evaluation  underscores  the  need  of   improving  educational \nframeworks  to  properly  integrate  AI  technology  into  dentistry  and  nursing  curriculum.  The  results \ndemonstrate that while a substantial number of  students have a basic comprehension of  AI, there are severe \nshortcomings in practical abilities and knowledge application. This disparity highlights the pressing need \nfor  educational  institutions  to  emphasize  AI  training,  concentrating  on  both  theoretical  principles  and \npractical applications. \n\nThe favorable sentiments shown by most students on AI suggest a willingness to use these technologies in \nclinical environments. Nonetheless, concerns about job displacement and the possible dehumanization of  \n\n13871 \n\n \n\fJournal of Ecohumanism \n2024 \nVolume: 3, No: 8, pp. 13868 \u2013 13875 \nISSN: 2752-6798 (Print) | ISSN 2752-6801 (Online) \nhttps://ecohumanism.co.uk/joe/ecohumanism  \nDOI: https://doi.org/10.62754/joe.v3i8.6513  \nmedical care persist, especially among students from economically poor families. Addressing these issues is \ncrucial for cultivating a supportive learning environment that promotes the ethical use of  AI in clinical \npractice. \n\nFuture research needs to concentrate on longitudinal studies to monitor shifts in knowledge and attitudes \namong healthcare professionals as AI becomes more integrated into practice. Furthermore, assessing the \nefficacy of  AI training on clinical outcomes will provide significant insights for curriculum improvement. \nBy providing students with a comprehensive awareness of  AI's potential and ethical considerations, the \nhealthcare industry can augment diagnostic precision and increase patient care. A collaborative strategy that \nintegrates the advantages of  AI with human experience is essential for progressing in dental radiology and \nmaintaining optimal patient care standards. \n\nReferences \n\n Larentzakis A, Lygeros N.
