outputs/journal_corpus_content.json:18561:  "venue": "Cureus Journal of Business and Economics.",
outputs/journal_corpus_content.json:18576:  "fulltext": "Cureus Journal of Business and Economics \n\nOpen Access Review Article\n\nBlessing and Curse: Reviewing the Implications of\nGenerative AI on Employee Relations in the\nReady-Made Garment Sector\n\nHong Chau Linh Nguyen   , Kudrat Khuda   , Dieu Hack-Polay \n\n2\n\n1\n\n3, 2\n\n1. Human Resource Management, Thuongmai University, Hanoi, VNM  2. Management, University of Lincoln, Lincoln,\nGBR 3. Management, Crandall University, Moncton, CAN\n\nReceived 06/10/2025 \n\nReview began 07/03/2025 \n\nReview ended 10/20/2025 \n\nPublished 11/13/2025\n\n\u00a9 Copyright 2025\n\nNguyen et al. This is an open access article\n\ndistributed under the terms of the Creative\n\nCommons Attribution License CC-BY 4.0.,\n\nCorresponding author: Kudrat Khuda, mkhuda@lincoln.ac.uk\n\nwhich permits unrestricted use, distribution,\n\nand reproduction in any medium, provided\n\nthe original author and source are credited.\n\nDOI:\n\nhttps://doi.org/10.7759/s44404-025-04991-8\n\nAbstract\nGenerative artificial intelligence (GAI) is transforming Human Resource Management, particularly\nEmployee Relations, by automating decision-making, enhancing personalization, and optimizing\noperational efficiency. This paper explores the implications of GAI adoption in the ready-made garments\n(RMG) sector, a labor-intensive industry in South and Southeast Asia. While GAI offers opportunities for\nproductivity gains, safety improvements, and enhanced communication, its integration raises concerns\nabout job displacement, skill gaps, and ethical dilemmas. Drawing on theoretical frameworks, such as the\nTechnology Acceptance Model, the Task-Technology Fit model, and Self-Determination Theory, this study\nexamines how GAI reshapes labor dynamics, organizational policies, and employee engagement. The\nfindings suggest that successful GAI adoption requires a balanced approach, incorporating training\ninitiatives, regulatory safeguards, and inclusive decision-making processes to mitigate risks and maximize\nbenefits. The study highlights the need for a nuanced, context-specific analysis of GAI\u2019s role in reshaping\nemployee relations within the RMG sector.\n\nCategories: Computational Intelligence and Information Management, Employee Relations & Labour Legislation,\nSustainable Human Resource\nKeywords: generative artificial intelligence, employee relations, ready-made garments, south asia, human resource\nmanagement\n\nIntroduction And Background\nGenerative artificial intelligence (GAI) is rapidly transforming Human Resource Management (HRM),\nparticularly Employee Relations (ER). This technology, which involves AI systems capable of generating\ncontents, solutions, and insights, is emerging as a powerful tool in reshaping how organizations manage\ntheir workforce. GAI refers to AI systems capable of automatically generating new content by identifying\nand leveraging patterns in extensive datasets (Andrieux et al., 2024). While traditional AI relied on human\nsupervision learning mechanism and relies on labeled data, GAI operates unsupervised, automatically\ndiscovering patterns, fostering creativity, and generating human-like contents (Fui-Hoon Nah et al., 2023)\n(Kronblad et al., 2024) (Agrawal, 2023). Due to high level of automation, GAI can create rapid reports,\ncustomize feedback, expediting decision-making processes (Kar et al., 2023), which is demonstrated in a\nhandful of prior literature and also practical reports (Feuerriegel et al., 2024) (Fui-Hoon Nah et al., 2023)\n(Gupta et al., 2024). This superior capability allows GAI to penetrate and change the way several sectors\noperate, including hospitality, healthcare, marketing, and education by advancing customer services,\nenabling personalization, and optimizing technical problem-solving (Brown et al., 2024) (Budhwar et al.,\n2023) (Kasneci et al., 2023). According to a 2023 McKinsey survey of more than 1,600 business leaders,\n55% reported that AI tools were invested in their work environment (McKinsey Company, 2023).\n\nDespite its remarkable features and potential for organizational uses, GAI has been criticized for ethical\nconcerns, including unfairness, data privacy violations, cybersecurity risks, misinformation, accountability\ngaps, and intellectual property issues (Walkowiak and Potts, 2024). To ensure ethical and fair adoption,\nGAI must adhere to key principles such as accountability, transparency, accuracy, and autonomy\n(Andrieux et al., 2024) (Rana et al., 2024). Given its diverse applications and requirements, from individual\nsupport to enhancing organizational productivity, as well as its distinct industrial characteristics, research\non GAI adoption should focus on specific contexts and workplace settings to address these complexities\neffectively. This paper aims to explore the implications of GAI adoption in the Ready-Made Garments\n(RMG) sector, a labor-intensive industry prevalent in South and Southeast Asia. The study seeks to\nprovide analysis of both the potential benefits and the challenges associated with integrating GAI into this\nsector.\n\nReview\nGAI adoption: theoretical perspectives\n\nHow to cite this article\nNguyen H, Khuda K, Hack-Polay D (November 13, 2025) Blessing and Curse: Reviewing the Implications of Generative AI on Employee Relations in\nthe Ready-Made Garment Sector . Cureus J Bus Econ 2 : es44404-025-04991-8. DOI https://doi.org/10.7759/s44404-025-04991-8\n\n \n\fCureus Journal of Business and Economics\n\nThe application of AI, and GAI specifically, in business has been widely examined through both micro and\nmacro lenses from the perspective of relatively popular theoretical frameworks (Chatterjee et al., 2021)\n(Manresa et al., 2024) (Agrawal, 2023). The paper drew on two main groups of articles: (1) scholarly\narticles covering the rationale and theoretical perspectives on the use of AI and (2) scholarly articles,\nthough limited, covering the emerging use of AI in the blue-collar sector. Along the two main criteria\nmentioned above, we focused on date, considering articles on technology adoption in the post 2000 era\nsince this where greater debate about AI issues emerged. Our main search was based on Google Scholar,\nwhich captures both scholarly and practice-based articles. The search revealed 150 articles that closely\nmatched our criteria. Proceeding by elimination based on area relevance, the researchers included only 70\nmore recent articles for the discussion (Figure 1).\n\nFIGURE 1: Flowchart for selection of articles\n\nSource: Author.\n\nAt the societal and organizational levels, previous authors investigated the succession of GAI adoption\nand acceptance by considering the uncertainty of external factors, such as legal environment,\norganizational regulations and procedures, infrastructures, and technological capabilities (Agrawal, 2023).\nConcerning external pressure, the Technology-Organizations-Environment (TOE) framework and\ninstitutional theory (DiMaggio and Powell, 1983) are theoretically driven for search in technology\nadoption and adaptation within the organizational context. The TOE framework examines the adoption of\nnew technologies by considering three key factors: technology itself, organizational characteristics, and\nthe external environment. But institutional theory emphasizes the role of government policies and\nenvironmental pressures in shaping organizational behavior and actions in adopting technology (Bag et\nal., 2021). More specifically, the Diffusion of Innovations theory  (Rogers, 1995) is often used to\ndemonstrate the process of how novel ideas and technology spread through different communication\nchannels within the organizations. This theory is frequently applied for actual and common GAI use such\nas ChatGPT, or real application of new technology like mobile phone ubiquitous system (Ahad and Busch,\n2023) (Gupta et al., 2024).\n\nAt the individual level, previous research seems to be over-reliant on  (Davis, 1989) technology acceptance\nmodel (TAM). TAM has been favored since it was developed in the information technology field with two\nconstructs, which are perceived ease of use and perceived usefulness. This theoretical model deeply\ninvestigates how individual readiness and decision-making play a hindering or driving role in technology\n\n2025 Nguyen et al. Cureus J Bus Econ 2 : es44404-025-04991-8. DOI https://doi.org/10.7759/s44404-025-04991-8\n\n2 of 12\n\n\fCureus Journal of Business and Economics\n\nadoption. Likewise, studies also approach the Technology Readiness Index (TRI) (Parasuraman, 2000),\nconcerning user technology acceptance (Flavi\u00e1n et al., 2021) (Manresa et al., 2024) to investigate how\nemployees are prepared for technological change. The concept of self-determination (SDT (Deci and Ryan,\n1985)), which is also commonly used in studies on GAI adoption, emphasizes the integration of\nemployees\u2019 needs and aspirations as well as intrinsic motivations into the value creation process (Ren and\nJackson, 2020) (Shen et al., 2024). As de Graaf (de Graaf, 2016) argues, how people perceive, respond, and\ninteract with technology can shape the concept of human-robot relationships.\n\nThese theoretical frameworks are often integrated (e.g., TAM-TOE, TAM-SDT, DoI-TOE) to examine both\nmacro- and micro-driven technology adoption (Ahad and Busch, 2023) (Chatterjee et al., 2021) (Shen et\nal., 2024). However, scholars argue that current theoretical frameworks on the application of new\ntechnology in organizations may pose challenges, considering the unique potential and capabilities of GAI\n(Brown et al., 2024) (Budhwar et al., 2023) (de Graaf, 2016). For example, both TAM and TOE studies often\npredict behavior rather than directly the outcomes of actual uses, which may not be valid assumptions in\nreal-world situations, as well as in various business contexts (Goodhue, 2007) (Kelly et al., 2023). (Sohn\nand Kwon, 2020) and (Gursoy et al., 2019) argue that theoretical frameworks cannot provide timely\noptimal predictions in the face of the rapid development of GAI devices.\n\nTheoretical frameworks are also limited in that they do not delve into the supporting factors that promote\nand complete a useful system with organizational settings, such as its design and suitability to the user's\npurpose, although these factors are important for both technology adoption and technology use to achieve\nhigh performance (Goodhue, 2007). These factors require much effort, and resources and depend on the\ntype of technology suitable for each type of field and business.\n\nGAI adoption and employee relations\n\nTaking a deeper look at human labor, the literature also demonstrates that GAI has significant impacts on\nER. The resource-based view (RBV) argues that organizations can leverage GAI as a valuable asset to\nenhance HRM by streamlining recruitment processes and improving training and development programs\n(Chowdhury et al., 2024) (Mohiuddin et al., 2022). GAI\u2019s ability to automatically generate content can\nsupport personalization, and frequently asked question processing for human services (e.g., monthly\nsalary, benefits, ongoing feedback, and suggestions, etc.), thereby promoting internal communications,\nemployee development and supporting organizations in making HR-related decisions (Feuerriegel et al.,\n2024) (Korzynski et al., 2024). Going beyond the conventional type of support resources, with a\nknowledge-based view (KBV), Malik et al. (Malik et al., 2023) emphasize the role of GAI in facilitating\nknowledge sharing and learning within the organization, especially by automating information\ndissemination and supporting collaborative knowledge building.\n\nSome researchers have focused on reassessing job roles as GAI integrates into HRM practices  (Boulus-\nR\u00f8dje et al., 2024) (Agrawal, 2023). Several studies have explored the Task-Technology Fit (TTF) model\n(Goodhue and Thompson, 1995), highlighting the importance of aligning technology capabilities with task\nrequirements. This alignment supports technology adoption and usage and enhances AI performance by\nensuring compatibility with workers\u2019 tasks (Ales et al., 2024) (Mikalef et al., 2019) (Agrawal, 2023). The\ntheory suggests that the more effectively a technology aligns with the tasks it supports, the greater its\nadoption and utility (Goodhue and Thompson, 1995) (Mikalef et al., 2019). Similarly, Howard and Rose\n(Howard and Rose, 2019) emphasize that a strong alignment between tasks and technology can\nsignificantly improve individual performance.\n\nBy aligning technology capabilities with task requirements, organizations can mitigate the risks\nassociated with automation and foster greater worker engagement. Additionally, from the perspective of\nSDT (Deci and Ryan, 1985), workers' positive interactions with GAI are shaped not only by task fit but also\nby intrinsic factors such as curiosity and the desire to learn. This suggests that both technical\ncompatibility and intrinsic motivation play critical roles in successful GAI adoption.\n\nTo optimize human-machine collaboration, Boulus-R\u00f8dje et al.  (Boulus-R\u00f8dje et al., 2024) identify three\nessential work requirements: articulation work (understanding AI functionalities), relationship work\n(fostering positive interactions with AI), and identity work (redefining professional roles). Their findings\nsuggest that GAI is evolving from being merely a tool to becoming a workplace partner, reshaping roles,\nworkflows, and professional identities. Workers navigate this transformation through phases of\nexperimentation, frustration, adaptation, and integration, ultimately enhancing performance while\npreserving professional integrity. Similarly, Kronblad et al. (Kronblad et al., 2024) highlight the acceptance\nof GAI as a partner, particularly among tech-savvy employees in professional service firms. These\nindividuals perceive GAI not as a threat but as a supportive tool that complements their work.\n\nThese approaches place employees at the core of business success, particularly in the context of the\ngrowing integration of GAI. As a result, GAI is increasingly regarded not merely as a tool but as a\ntransformative factor that redefines the employment relationship, fostering greater employee engagement\nwith these technologies (Ryan and Deci, 2017) (Shen et al., 2024).\n\n2025 Nguyen et al. Cureus J Bus Econ 2 : es44404-025-04991-8. DOI https://doi.org/10.7759/s44404-025-04991-8\n\n3 of 12\n\n\fCureus Journal of Business and Economics\n\nWhile viewing GAI as a tool to harmonize human labor and technology in the workplace, by automating\nroutine tasks, streamlining operations, and reducing conflict (Budhwar et al., 2023) (Dong et al., 2024)\n(Malik et al., 2023), may offer practical benefits, this perspective risks adopting a unitarist bias. Unitarism,\nwhich promotes the rationalization of human-machine cooperation to achieve common organizational\ngoals, is often considered an ideal framework for fostering harmonious labor relations (Kaufman et al.,\n2020). Within HRM and organizational behavior research, the unitarist view dominates, presenting\nhuman-machine collaboration as a means to align workforce efforts with organizational objectives (Barry\nand Wilkinson, 2021) (Wilkinson et al., 2020). Studies on AI's impact on human labor frequently echo this\nidealized perspective. For instance, a review of 103 studies by Intahchomphoo et al. (Intahchomphoo et\nal., 2024) revealed a predominant focus on AI's positive effects, with only 37 studies addressing its\npotential negative implications for workers.\n\nHowever, such unitarist frameworks often overlook the contextual nuances of different sectors, potentially\nleading to overly optimistic projections for GAI adoption (Chowdhury et al., 2024). They may also fail to\naccount for conflicts arising in decision-making influenced by GAI or exacerbate existing inequalities in\npluralistic (diverse interest groups) or extreme (conflicting interests) labor relations (Kaufman et al.,\n2020). While GAI is often celebrated for its efficiency and innovation, we have made a point to highlight\nthe less visible tensions that come with its adoption. Instead of merely assuming it\u2019s a one-size-fits-all\nsolution, we have pointed to risks like exclusion and uneven accesses, especially in sectors like RMG. By\nbringing in alternative viewpoints, we aim to offer a more grounded and realistic take on what GAI means\nfor workers and organizations alike. To address these gaps, Brown et al. (Brown et al., 2024) emphasize the\nnecessity of investigating GAI adoption through a cultural and contextual lens, with particular attention\nto workforce characteristics. Similarly, Wamba et al. (Wamba et al., 2023) argue that organizations cannot\nachieve optimal performance without adequately addressing external and environmental pressures.\nMoreover, TTF model underscores that effective GAI application requires alignment not only between task\nrequirements and technological features but also with individual abilities and traits. While the previously\nmentioned papers primarily focus on GAI adoption among white-collar workers or managerial-level\nemployees (Manresa et al., 2024), this highlights the increasing need for a careful analysis of sector-\nspecific contexts, organizational dynamics, and the influence of individual characteristics on GAI\nimplementation.\n\nGAI and ER relation in RMG settings\n\nThe RMG sector is a cornerstone of the export-driven economies of developing South and Southeast Asian\ncountries, including Bangladesh, India, Pakistan, China, Vietnam, and Cambodia. Despite significant\ngrowth in recent years, the fast fashion phenomenon and the demand for mass production have placed\nincreasing pressure on RMG manufacturers. These pressures include the need to accelerate production\ncycles, respond swiftly to consumer demands, and maintain competitive production costs (Berg et al.,\n2021) (Gangoda et al., 2023). As a result, environmental and labor challenges have become critical issues\nwithin the sector.\n\nBoth academic researchers and industry practitioners emphasize the potential of technological\nadvancements to mitigate these challenges. Innovations in production prediction, management, and\noperational control could enable textile organizations to manage large order requirements more\nefficiently (Gangoda et al., 2023). Similarly, GAI has gained recognition as a valuable tool for addressing\noperational inefficiencies in the RMG and broader textile sectors (Ghobakhloo et al., 2024) (Sikka et al.,\n2022). The integration of AI and automation into the RMG sector not only enhances machine efficiency\nbut also optimizes overall production operations while controlling costs (Intahchomphoo et al., 2024)\n(Sikka et al., 2022).\n\nGAI, in particular, is revolutionizing operational practices with its self-learning and creative capabilities.\nIt enables algorithmic fashion design, facilitating rapid prototyping, customization, and increase (Hasan\net al., 2020) productivity, all while reducing time-to-market (Salman et al., 2023). Additionally, GAI\nsupports sustainability initiatives by improving supply chain processes and reducing waste, a pressing\nconcern amid the growth of fast fashion and increased consumption (Ghobakhloo et al., 2024) (Hossain et\nal., 2024) (Mohiuddin et al., 2022) (Sikka et al., 2022).\n\nHowever, as discussed earlier, focusing solely on the technological benefits of GAI may overlook the\nnuances of sector-specific and labor force characteristics (Brown et al., 2024) (Chowdhury et al., 2024).\nThe RMG sector, traditionally labor-intensive, has relied heavily on low-cost labor as a competitive\nadvantage (De Mattos et al., 2021) (Hasan et al., 2020). Yet, a critical question arises: Is cheap labor still a\nsustainable competitive advantage for these countries in an AI-driven world? It can be seen that the\ntransition to GAI requires a rethinking of labor strategies and an adaptation of organizational structures.\n\nScholars have argued that adopting new technologies in the RMG sector requires more than just financial\ninvestment in advanced infrastructure (Calabrese et al., 2020) (Saha et al., 2024) (Salman et al., 2023).\nIt necessitates strengthening regulatory frameworks (Calabrese et al., 2020), ensuring compliance with\nethical standards, and addressing organizational resistance to change. While research on employee\n\n2025 Nguyen et al. Cureus J Bus Econ 2 : es44404-025-04991-8. DOI https://doi.org/10.7759/s44404-025-04991-8\n\n4 of 12\n\n\fCureus Journal of Business and Economics\n\nrelations in the RMG sector has traditionally focused on organizational compliance with ethical standards\n(Azim et al., 2020) (Uddin et al., 2021), these steps are now crucial in managing the shift from labor-\nintensive practices to more technology-driven models. Without adequate support policies, however,\nemployees may experience dissatisfaction and disengagement, which can lower morale and undermine\nproductivity. Therefore, insufficient policy support not only affects individual satisfaction but also\nhampers the broader transformation needed to redefine labor dynamics in the age of automation.\n\nThese dynamics highlight the need for a pluralist approach that carefully considers the diverse interests of\nstakeholders, including workers, employers, and policymakers. As Wamba et al. (Wamba et al., 2023) note,\noptimal organizational performance cannot be achieved without addressing external pressures and\ncontextual challenges. Therefore, any examination of GAI's role in the RMG sector must move beyond\nunitarist narratives to not only investigate socioeconomic context but also its workforce dynamics.\n\nThe positive impact of GAI on ER in RMG\n\nIncreasing Safety and Reducing Health Hazards\n\nGAI and robotics have the potential to address labor safety issues in the manufacturing sector by\nautomating operational tasks, such as pruning, handling heavy objects, and performing hazardous jobs\n(Intahchomphoo et al., 2024). This automation can mitigate the longstanding challenges of poor\noccupational safety and health, which are inherent in the garment manufacturing industry (Rubel et al.,\n2016). As a result, the adoption of GAI technologies may enhance workplace safety and improve workers'\njob satisfaction and overall sense of security.\n\nFurthermore, the emergence of GAI in the RMG sector potentially introduces the concept of \"blue collar\nwith a tie\" (Meisinger, 2022). The authors (ibid.) emphasize the surprising insight that, despite a potential\ndecline in demand for traditional blue-collar workers, this labor segment is progressively transitioning\ninto more prestigious and non-routine roles redefining itself under this new paradigm (Reijnders and De\nVries, 2018).\n\nEmployee Wellbeing\n\nWhile traditional AI systems face considerable limitations in managing complex, nuanced interactions\nthat require human expertise (Andrieux et al., 2024), GAI offers innovative solutions to address such\nchallenges. For instance, GAI-powered chatbots have demonstrated their utility as cognitive behavioral\nsupport tools, providing emotional assistance and personalized interactions (Budhwar et al., 2023)\n(Fitzpatrick et al., 2017). These applications not only support mental health but also contribute to creating\na more fulfilling and individualized working environment. Consequently, GAI can play a significant role in\nimproving workplace conditions and employee satisfaction (Hasan et al., 2020) (Syed and Mahmud, 2022).\n\nUnexpected Advantages from Workers\u2019 Characteristics\n\nSurprisingly, the characteristics of the RMG workforce, especially female workers, can serve as an\nadvantage in promoting employee satisfaction in digital context. According to Asadullah and Talukder\n(Asadullah and Talukder, 2019), the satisfaction of female workers in the RMG sector depends not only on\nthe working environment but also on external social and cultural factors. Their research indicates that\nwomen working in environments where most of their colleagues are female tend to be more satisfied, even\nif the working conditions are less than ideal. Additionally, monetary factors are often significant drivers of\njob satisfaction for RMG workers (Dey and Basak, 2017) (Syed and Mahmud, 2022). In this context, their\nperceived satisfaction and well-being play a significant role in their overall job satisfaction.\n\nBender and Roche (Bender and Roche, 2016) found that female workers often have lower expectations for\ncertain job attributes, such as job flexibility. This may explain why, despite the RMG industry not offering\nsufficient flexibility, female workers are still able to maintain satisfaction. For example, in Bangladesh,\nwhere women in the RMG sector have few alternative employment options, the gap between their\nexpectations and job performance is often narrower than for men, leading to higher levels of satisfaction\n(Fui-Hoon Nah et al., 2023). Therefore, increasing awareness of these employee demographics,\nparticularly in the context of digital work, may offer distinct advantages over their higher-expectation\ncounterparts in enhancing satisfaction and fostering a more positive attitude toward GAI adoption.\n\nChallenges for RMG Workforce\n\nThe integration of AI technologies into the RMG sector presents considerable challenges, particularly in\nrelation to labor concerns. Given the unique nature of the RMG workforce, which is adapting to rapid\ntechnological advancements, it is vital to evaluate how businesses and workers can navigate the labor\nrelation challenges and risks posed by the adoption of GAI (Hasan et al., 2020). These challenges are\nparticularly evident in areas such as job displacement, gender inequities in the workplace, and skill\n\n2025 Nguyen et al. Cureus J Bus Econ 2 : es44404-025-04991-8. DOI https://doi.org/10.7759/s44404-025-04991-8\n\n5 of 12\n\n\fCureus Journal of Business and Economics\n\ndeficits.\n\nJob Replacement\n\nAccording to Makridakis (Makridakis, 2017), the rise of emerging AI technologies continues to drive a\ndecline in manufacturing employment, resulting in profound changes to businesses and jobs. Akter et al.\n(Akter et al., 2024) emphasize that automation poses a significant threat to job security, with projections\nsuggesting that up to 60% of jobs could be affected by 2041. The COVID-19 pandemic has further\nexacerbated tensions in labor relations within the RMG industry, leading to more severe layoffs and\nincreased job insecurity (Hardy et al., 2024). This trend has heightened the vulnerability of informal and\nunprotected (Hardy et al., 2024), particularly among female workers, who face disproportionate caregiving\nburdens (Dey and Basak, 2017). As Ehsan (Ehsan, 2021) notes, women in RMG manufacturing are often\nexcluded from decision-making roles, which results in their relegation to lower-paid and less secure\npositions, especially when it comes to the implementation of GAI.\n\nWhile technology can offer peace of mind by automating certain processes and addressing traditionally\nphysical, psychological, and emotional challenges, it also introduces new sources of stress, such as job\nuncertainty and the possibility of job loss (Budhwar et al., 2022) (Budhwar et al., 2023). Prior research has\nfound that in developing economies, technological advancements significantly impact employment,\nheightening job insecurity and generating new technology-related well-being concerns, such as\nautomation anxiety or fear of job loss (Bertulfo et al., 2019) (Dong et al., 2024) (Islam, 2018). This\npsychological strain significantly affects the female workforce, the most vulnerable group in the RMG\nindustry.\n\nMoreover, unlike other white-collar industries, the role of GAI, when combined with human labor, is likely\nto challenge the concept of \"decent work\" in the RMG sector due to the risk of unequal distribution of\nbenefits and burdens as AI reshapes work roles (Budhwar et al., 2023) (De Mattos et al., 2021). The\nInternational Labor Organization defines \"decent work\" as including job creation, social protection, rights\nat work, and social dialogue, advocating for fair wages, safe working conditions, workers' rights, and job\nsecurity (Khan et al., 2019). In the RMG sector, job creation is crucial for poverty reduction, especially in\ndeveloping countries. As AI, and later GAI, gradually reshape work roles, it is essential to ensure that these\nchanges align with the principles of decent work and protect workers at risk of displacement. Therefore,\nidentifying the types of technology-related tasks, according to the framework of Boulus-R\u00f8dje et al.\n(Boulus-R\u00f8dje et al., 2024) for RMG workers and implementing appropriate training initiatives will be key\nin helping them adapt to these new roles.\n\nSkill Gaps and Resistance to Change\n\nThe predominantly low-skilled workforce in the RMG sector, which includes a significant underemployed\nand unemployed female demographic, may face significant barriers in adopting automation and advanced\nmachinery (Ehsan, 2021) (Islam, 2018). According to Salman et al.  (Salman et al., 2023), the most\nsignificant barriers to adopting Industry 4.0 in Bangladesh's RMG sector are \"I4.0 training,\" \"lack of\nmotivation,\" and \"resistance to change,\" based on their prominence scores. Likewise, in a literature review\npaper, Calabrese et al. (Calabrese et al., 2020) indicate that the lack of a skilled and specialized labor force,\nas well as insufficient knowledge, skills, attitudes, and awareness regarding emerging technologies,\nfurther exacerbates these challenges in developing countries' RMG sectors.\n\nAdditionally, RMG manufacturers lack investment in technology education (Saha et al., 2024); therefore,\nfemale workers face fewer opportunities for training and skill development due to job characteristics that\ndo not support on-the-job training. Bertulfo et al. (Bertulfo et al., 2019) also highlight that limited access\nto training programs for female RMG workers not only hampers their ability to adapt to new technologies\nbut also increases frustration and disengagement.\n\nThis situation poses significant challenges for the RMG industry to implement appropriate upskilling and\nreskilling program, which relies heavily on aligning its operations with the geographic, cultural, and low-\neducation contexts of rural workers (Akter et al., 2024). Overcoming these barriers requires a strategic\nfocus on enhancing knowledge, providing skill training, and raising awareness to foster readiness for\ntechnological transformation.\n\nOpportunities for addressing ER challenges in RMG\n\nTraining Initiatives\n\nConsidering the increasing challenges of job displacement in the RMG sector, training is seen as an\neffective approach to upskill workers and ensure they possess the necessary skills to work with emerging\ntechnologies (Ooi et al., 2023). Training should not only enhance technical abilities but also foster a sense\nof control and self-efficacy, promoting long-term engagement and success in the workplace (Al Naqbi et\n\n2025 Nguyen et al. Cureus J Bus Econ 2 : es44404-025-04991-8. DOI https://doi.org/10.7759/s44404-025-04991-8\n\n6 of 12\n\n\fCureus Journal of Business and Economics\n\nal., 2024). From the perspective of SDT, worker satisfaction and adaptation to GAI depend on fulfilling\npsychological needs such as autonomy and competence (Deci et al., 2017).\n\nAlthough the implementation of GAI presents a skill gap challenge for RMG employees, there is clear\nevidence that it offers a significant opportunity to alleviate workforce training constraints and reduce\ninequality within the sector. While there is a gap in learning how to use GAI, its features can help\nemployees bridge this gap by utilizing GAI as a tool to enhance their skills and knowledge. According to\nKorzynski et al. (Korzynski et al., 2024), personalized learning via GAI can be instrumental in bridging the\nskills gap, especially for female workers, who are often overlooked for higher-skilled positions in favor of\ntheir male counterparts (Akter et al., 2024). Wiles et al.  (Wiles et al., 2024) argue that reskilling through\nGAI enables less-skilled workers to take on more complex tasks.\n\nIn this regard, GAI may become an \"exoskeleton,\" offering temporary skill support, rather than a substitute\nfor continuous training that keeps pace with technological advancements (Wiles et al., 2024). Akter et al.\n(Akter et al., 2024) address this concern by proposing training initiatives tailored to the specific needs of\nrural workers, with language, teaching methods, and content customized for better accessibility.\nFurthermore, (Gangoda et al., 2023) Gangoda et al. argue that training is essential for retaining valuable\ntalent in the garment sector. Based on interviews with 21 participants across five countries: Sri Lanka, the\nUnited States, China, Hong Kong, and India, these authors suggest that bridging the skill gap requires\ndeveloping specializations in emerging fields, such as AI and 3D technology, within the garment industry.\nThis would help promote more effective and continuous internal training.\n\nTraining is argued as a crucial solution for facilitating the adoption of GAI in the RMG sector, helping to\novercome resistance to change and boosting workers' confidence in using new technologies (Gangoda et\nal., 2023) (Guo et al., 2024)\n\nTo achieve sustainable effectiveness, flexible, continuous, and workforce-specific training strategies are\nessential. Managers in RMG may also need to strike a balance between the benefits of automation and the\nneed to enhance the social-emotional competencies of workers (Smet et al., 2024), ultimately fostering a\nmore equitable and productive work environment.\n\n Increase Job Satisfaction Through Employee Trust\n\nMany scholars emphasize that in the digital age, increasing employee satisfaction and engagement\nrequires cultivating strong trust in technology (Glikson and Woolley, 2020) (Marimon et al., 2024)\n(Agrawal, 2023). Low trust in technology can lead to underuse and inefficiency, while excessive trust in\nineffective technology can result in misuse and safety risks (Glikson and Woolley, 2020). Research shows\nthat employees with an open attitude toward new technology not only work more efficiently but also feel\nmore engaged in their work (Malik et al., 2023) (Shen et al., 2024). The TAM), later extended with a trust\nvariable, indicates that trust is a key predictor of both behavioral intention and satisfaction with new\ntechnology (Park and Yoon, 2024) (Prasad and De, 2024). In corporate settings, when employees perceive\nGAI as trustworthy, fair, and transparent, they not only accept it but also appreciate the value it brings\n(Zerilli et al., 2022). These authors also found that when users trust the accuracy and safety of AI tools,\nthey are more likely to use them again and feel more satisfied with the experience (Park and Yoon, 2024)\n(Prasad and De, 2024) (Zerilli et al., 2022).\n\nEncouraging employees is also essential for strengthening this trust and, in turn, enhancing satisfaction.\nAccording to a survey of 500 U.S. employees, Dong et al. (Dong et al., 2024) conclude that organizations\nthat encourage employee participation in GAI-related decisions have successfully increased both\nemployee satisfaction and adoption. When employees feel their voices are respected, they tend to perceive\ntechnology as a supportive tool rather than a threat. This reduces concerns about job loss and bias while\nfostering collaboration between humans and GAI. Furthermore, trust helps minimize the psychological\ndistance between employees and technology. In the workplace, building trust in GAI through transparency\nand accountability can create a positive cycle of trust, acceptance, and satisfaction. According to Budhwar\net al. (Budhwar et al., 2022) and Dong et al. (Dong et al., 2024), employee trust in AI systems depends\nlargely on transparency and fairness in their operation. Intahchomphoo et al. (Intahchomphoo et al.,\n2024) argue that addressing concerns about ethics, privacy, and bias is crucial for trust in GAI to become a\npowerful driver of a harmonious, sustainable, and productive workplace.\n\nTo help guide future research, we have also included a summary table (Table 1) in the Appendices that\noutlines five key areas where further investigation is needed. These include job displacement, gendered\nimpacts, training and upskilling, ethical considerations, and employee trust. Each theme is paired with a\nrelevant research question and selected references to support deeper exploration of how GAI is reshaping\nemployee relations in the RMG sector.\n\nConclusions\n\n2025 Nguyen et al. Cureus J Bus Econ 2 : es44404-025-04991-8. DOI https://doi.org/10.7759/s44404-025-04991-8\n\n7 of 12\n\n\fCureus Journal of Business and Economics\n\nThe research has implications for HR practice in blue-collar industries in developing economies. It shows\nthe need for the field to rise up to the challenges and opportunities of the use of AI in a sector\ntraditionally perceived to be a low-skilled area. The application of AI can help HR to understand details of\nthe work environment and productivity issues, which if resolved, can enhance organizations\u2019 social\nresponsibility. GAI has the potential to revolutionize various industries by enhancing efficiency,\nproductivity, and innovation. However, implementing AI in sectors where it has not been adopted yet\npresents several challenges. These challenges range from data availability and quality to ethical and\nprivacy concerns. Implementing generative AI in HRM presents several challenges that organizations\nmust navigate to fully leverage its potential. In particular, the RMG industry faces workforce challenges,\nincluding labor-intensive processes, skill shortages, and evolving consumer demands. Generative AI, with\nits ability to automate tasks, enhance creativity, and optimize supply chains, can transform employment\nrelations. However, it also raises concerns related to job displacement, discrimination, and employer\nliability. The paper\u2019s main limitation is to draw solely on literature without further empirical data.\nHowever, it contributes to the literature on the increasing use of technology in the RMG sector, an area of\nimportance that is little explored. This analysis can inform emerging research since it has compiled and\nsystematically examined the limited research on the topic, which can provide direction for future research.\n\nThis study is based solely on a review of existing literature, without the support of primary empirical data.\nAs such, while it offers a comprehensive synthesis of current research and theoretical perspectives, it may\nnot fully capture the lived experiences or nuanced realities of workers and organizations within the RMG\nsector. Additionally, the reliance on secondary sources may limit the generalizability of findings across\ndifferent national contexts. Future research would benefit from incorporating fieldwork, cross-country\ncomparisons, and direct engagement with stakeholders to validate and expand upon the insights\npresented.\n\nAppendices\n\nResearch area\n\nPromising research question\n\nExemplary references\n\nGAI and job displacement\n\nHow can GAI adoption be balanced with job preservation in labor-intensive\n\nsectors?\n\n(Makridakis, 2017); (Akter et al., 2024)\n\nGendered impact of GAI\n\nWhat are the gender-specific implications of GAI in the RMG workforce?\n\n(Ehsan, 2021); (Dey and Basak, 2017)\n\nTraining and upskilling\n\nWhat models of GAI-supported training are most effective for low-skilled\n\n(Wiles et al., 2024); (Korzynski et al.,\n\nworkers?\n\n2024)\n\nEthical and regulatory\n\nHow can ethical standards be embedded in GAI deployment in developing\n\nframeworks\n\neconomies?\n\nEmployee trust and\n\nengagement\n\nWhat factors foster trust in GAI among blue-collar workers?\n\n(Andrieux et al., 2024); (Rana et al., 2024)\n\n(Glikson and Woolley, 2020); (Dong et al.,\n\n2024)\n\nTABLE 1: Future research directions on GAI and employee relations in the RMG sector\n\nGAI, generative artificial intelligence; RMG, ready-made garment.\n\nAdditional Information\nAuthor Contributions\n\nAll authors have reviewed the final version to be published and agreed to be accountable for all aspects of\nthe work.\n\nConcept and design:  Kudrat Khuda, Hong Chau Linh Nguyen\n\nAcquisition, analysis, or interpretation of data:   Kudrat Khuda, Dieu Hack-Polay, Hong Chau Linh\nNguyen\n\nDrafting of the manuscript:  Kudrat Khuda, Hong Chau Linh Nguyen\n\nCritical review of the manuscript for important intellectual content:  Kudrat Khuda, Dieu Hack-Polay\n\nSupervision:  Kudrat Khuda\n\nDisclosures\n\n2025 Nguyen et al.
